Stochastic Variational Inference on Bayesian

==PAGE:2==

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
We consider ways of learning Bayesian models eﬃciently when the variables partition into
a ﬁnite set of global model parameters and an unbounded collection of local parameters,
scaling in the size of the problem. In particular, we consider how large-scale models with
many local parameters can circumvent the slow co-adaptation of the two variable sets,
using the ideas of stochastic variational inference (SVI) presented by Hoﬀman et al. [20].
Application-wise we focus on nonparametric relational models with a particular focus
on the Inﬁnite Relational Model (IRM) [30].
We establish the probabilistic machinery of exponential families and information
geometry necessary to implement the ideas of SVI and detail how nonparametric distri-
butions can be used to model networks in a very general sense. We further show how
variational inference can be used to approximatively solve the problem of Bayesian infer-
ence eﬃciently, and how it is made even more eﬃcient by the introduction of stochastic
gradient approximations.
We further expand on some of the ideas on sampling presented in [17]. We use a
possibly novel scheme to analyze the inﬂuence the sampling has on the variance and
ﬁnd that the potential gain from the existing scheme is minor in our case, something we
later demonstrate.
We also consider an alternative approach of marginalizing out the model parameters
to completely circumvent the co-adaptation issue. This approach introduces a number
of new issues though, and we illustrate some potential solutions.
In conclusion we ﬁnd that SVI as ﬁrst presented is a poor match for the IRM due to
its conditional independence structure, but that it is amenable to a direct treatment by
stochastic natural gradients. We analyze the complexity of this approach compared to
standard inference and ﬁnd that we can go from O(|E|T + NT 2) to only O(NT 2) for a
graph with N vertices and |E| edges. This is a considerable advantage if the edges grow
faster than O(N), but only a marginal advantage for sparse graphs unfortunately.
Finally, we implement the majority of the algorithms presented and empirically verify
our claims.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Networks and hierarchies are everywhere around us, from the ubiquitous social networks,
over the distribution networks of shipping and telecommunication providers, to the com-
mand hierarchies of companies. Many things could be argued to be deﬁned better by
their relations than by their properties, and this is one of the underlying motivations for
network science.
But why do networks look like they do? A plethora of diﬀerent mathematical models
have been introduced over the last couple of decades in an attempt to explain network
formation and structure[26, 51]. For this thesis, we want to approach the problem from
a machine learning perspective and model it using a probabilistic model.
In probabilistic modeling we place probability distributions on our objects of inquiry,
networks for instance, and reﬂect on the likelihood of a network being generated by a
particular model. There are two possible end results using this type of modeling: either
we recover the single most likely model, or we recover a new probability distribution
over the most likely models. The latter case is referred to as Bayesian learning, and is
the approach we will take here.
Bayesian learning leads to powerful weighted mixture models, but at the cost of often
being hard to train. Researchers are often forced to use slow and diﬃcult to validate
Markov Chain Monte Carlo models [16]. We will instead look at an alternative method
known as variational inference which is faster at the cost of cutting some corners by
working with approximations [56]. Yet even these faster methods have problems coping
with the truly massive networks that are of interest to researchers. What we need is a
more scalable method of Bayesian learning.
This thesis takes its oﬀset in a recent article by Hoﬀman et al. [20] which details a new
scalable framework for Bayesian learning named stochastic variational inference which
combines ideas from stochastic optimization, information geometry, and variational in-
ference. Two follow-up articles further detailed how these ideas could be applied to
network modeling, which incited us to pursue this idea further [17, 31].
Where the two articles focused on the “mixed-membership” class of network models
[2] another particularly ubiquitous probabilistic model is the stochastic block model
which posits that networks form by communities engaging with each other. We will try
applying stochastic variational inference to a particular extension of the block model
which circumvents the problem that the number of communities is unknown a priori,
which usually means that researchers have to go through a grueling amount of model
selection.
To get around this issue, we turn to nonparametric distributions which are gaining
some traction in the machine learning community. Nonparametric distributions turn
==PAGE:10==
2
1 Introduction
out to have tight connections to networks in particular [43], and have been applied with
success to network modeling on several occasions [31, 11, 30]. Their main advantage is
that they are not restricted to a ﬁnite number of parameters (thus the name) and can
adapt the model structure itself to ﬁt the observed data.
In the case of stochastic block models, transferring it to the nonparametric domain
leads to a potentially inﬁnite number of communities allowing it to create and collapse
communities as data is introduced. This variant is called the inﬁnite relational model
[30] and will be the primary focus of this thesis.
Our goals are as follows:
• Explore the theoretical foundations of Bayesian nonparametric relational models,
with a particular focus on the inﬁnite relational model and its hindrances to eﬃ-
cient inference.
• Attempt to adapt or otherwise incorporate the ideas of Hoﬀman et al.’s stochastic
variational inference to the inﬁnite relational model.
• Investigate other ways to exploit the model structure of relational models for eﬃ-
cient inference.
In addition, we will produce an implementation of the algorithms in Python so that
we can empirically verify our claims.
These goals lead to a thesis with the following structure:
Preliminaries in this chapter we will detail the necessary background material, cover-
ing elements of Bayesian learning, advanced probability theory, optimization, and
nonparametric distributions.
Variational Inference next, we go into detail with the theory and methodology of
variational inference and its various possible extensions.
Relational Models here, we document network models in general, and cover the block
model family and the mixed-membership family in particular detail.
Variational Inference for Relational Models with both models and inference pro-
cedures in place, we show how to combine them to make learning algorithms for
the graph models covered in the previous chapter.
Implementation we take a brief detour to discuss scalability and how our learning
approaches match up against each other, as well as how the algorithm was imple-
mented and validated.
Results in this chapter we bring our algorithms to bear on synthetic and real-world
problems to document their applicability and their properties.
Conclusion ﬁnally, we discuss our results in relation to our initial hypotheses and
comment on possible extensions.
==PAGE:11==
CHAPTER 2
Preliminaries
Before we cover variational inference and relational models in more detail, we need to
establish the mathematical foundations we will be relying on.
We will discuss a number of high-level subjects that all relate to variational and
relational inference.
Bayesian Statistics at its core, this thesis is about solving a particular instance of the
general Bayesian inference problem so we will start by establishing exactly what
this problem is. We will here primarily lean on the classic textbooks [6, 16].
Exponential Family it turns out that variational inference ﬁts extremely well with
the so-called exponential family of probability distributions. We will investigate
their properties to determine why they are a good match. Our primary reference
here will be the survey [56].
Optimization a common machine learning design pattern is to construct a model of
the data, deﬁne an objective function which deﬁnes how well the model ﬁts the
data, and then optimizing said objective [14].
variational inference is a prime
example of this approach, and we will thus beneﬁt from considering optimization
in the general setting. We will here use the textbook by Nocedal and Wright [42]
as much as possible, except for stochastic variational inference where we will use
a number of articles [46, 9, 8].
Nonparametrics we cover the basics of nonparametric distributions here as their ﬂex-
ibility lends itself quite well to the analysis of graphs. We will primarily reference
the excellent survey by [43], but we will also on occasion reference an unpublished
manuscript by Jordan and Teh [28].
Let us now begin by deﬁning the particular problem we want to solve.
2.1
Bayesian Statistics
Statisticians and data scientists often talk of the dichotomy between the frequentist and
the Bayesian paradigm. While the paradigms depend on the same underlying mathemat-
ics, they oﬀer diﬀerent interpretations. In short, frequentists consider the probability of
an event to be a sort of asymptotic frequency of said event given repeated experiments,
while Bayesians interpret probability as belief that a quantity will take a particular value.
Mathematically, belief and probability follow the same axioms and have the same prop-
erties[16, 6], but a strict frequentist interpretation imposes limits on what objects can be
==PAGE:12==
4
2 Preliminaries
considered, and manipulated, as random. Namely, unknown but ﬁxed quantities, such
as model parameters underlying some particular set of observations, does not qualify
as a random variable in the frequentist sense, but can be modeled with a probabilistic
distribution under the Bayesian paradigm as we can have varying belief in the value
of the variable. These concerns are mostly academic and philosophical, and frequentist
approaches can generally be applied to Bayesian random variables and vice versa, but
there is a qualitative diﬀerence in how they model problems. In particular, where a
frequentist will often look for the most likely solution, a Bayesian approach leads to a
distribution over all solutions. For this paper, we will focus on the Bayesian paradigm in
the context of machine learning, where we want to arrive at a distribution over models
given a set of observed data.
2.1.1
Bayes’ Theorem
Bayes’ theorem is a surprisingly simple yet remarkably inﬂuential theorem which lends
its name to the entire branch of Bayesian statistics. Its own namesake is the reverend
Thomas Bayes who is, perhaps a bit unfairly, considered the father of the ﬁeld [6].
Brieﬂy, Bayes’ theorem can be stated as follows [6].
Theorem 2.1.1 (Bayes Theorem). For random variables A and B, the conditional
distributions p(B|A) and p(A|B) are related by
p(B|A) = p(A|B)p(B)
p(A)
= p(A|B)p(B)
p(A)
(2.1)
Proof. this follows rather straightforwardly from the chain rule, if we note that we can
choose the order of decomposition freely:
p(A, B) = p(A|B)p(B) = p(B|A)p(A)
(2.2)
dividing by p(B) we recover Bayes’ Theorem.
Bayes’ Theorem gives us a formula for inverting a conditional distribution. This is
especially useful in machine learning when we are working with probabilistic models
which give us the probability of a particular observation conditioned on a set of model
parameters.
Bayes’ Theorem then allows us to ﬁnd the distribution over the model
parameters conditioned on the data, which is essentially the whole inference problem.
2.1.2
Bayesian Inference
Let us consider a machine learning problem where we have deﬁned a probabilistic model
p(x|θ) which describes the probability of an observation x given a parameter setting θ.
Let us further assume that we are working in the Bayesian paradigm, so that θ can be
considered a random variable in its own right. Finally, let us assume we have a dataset
==PAGE:13==
2.2 The Exponential Family
5
D of observations. Given data and a model, what we want to recover is the probability
over parameters θ, written as p(θ|D). We can apply Bayes’ theorem to state:
p(θ|D) = p(D|θ)p(θ)
p(D)
=
p(D|θ)p(θ)
∫
p(D|θ)p(θ) dθ.
(2.3)
Let us deﬁne the terminology we will use. The function p(D|θ) is denoted the likelihood
function. This comes from the broader deﬁnition of a likelihood function as a condi-
tional distribution where we ﬁx the argument and consider the condition to be the free
variable. The consequence is that the likelihood function is not in general a probability
distribution when considered as a function of θ. This is why it is necessary to introduce
the normalization term p(D). This term is denoted the marginal likelihood (since we
have marginalized out the model parameters) or the model evidence. We should note
that given a ﬁxed dataset, the evidence is a constant given the underlying probabilistic
model structure.1
The inference problem is the question of how you identity plausible parameter set-
tings. In the Bayesian setting, the problem is explicitly solved by equation (2.3) which
places probability mass on each parameter setting proportional to the belief that the
data was generated from said setting. The numerator is simply the joint distribution, so
inference reduces to computing the model evidence p(D). Unfortunately, this is an often
intractable problem. The integral in equation (2.3) only rarely has an analytical solu-
tion, and since Bayesian problems often have a high dimensionality, classic numerical
methods such as quadrature are often unstable[6].
2.2
The Exponential Family
Throughout this report we will rely on a powerful yet remarkably general family of
distributions known as the exponential family. Every member of this family takes the
following general form [56].
Deﬁnition 2.2.1 (exponential family distribution). An exponential family distribution
p(x; η) over x ∈Ωwith natural parameters η has the general form
p(x; η) = h(x) exp(η⊤T(x) −A(η)),
x ∈Ω,
η ∈Θ
(2.4)
where h(x) is the base measure, T(x) : Ω→Θ is a function of suﬃcient statistics, and
A(η) : Θ →R+ is the log-normalizer.
The deﬁnition should make it transparent why it is called the exponential family. To
really understand the deﬁnition, we need to get to grips with the diﬀerent components.
base measure h(x) the base probability measure is the part of the distribution which
is independent of the parameters. It is often constant.
1one can consider all distributions in the modeling process to be implicitly conditioned on the model
choice M.
==PAGE:14==
6
2 Preliminaries
Suﬃcient statistics T(x) x is only evaluated through the function T : Ω→Θ which
maps the observation x to K suﬃcient statistics. The suﬃcient statistics deﬁne
what qualities of the a sample are actually measurable and recoverable.
log-normalizer A(η) the ﬁnal term normalizes the function to deﬁne a proper proba-
bility distribution:
A(η) = ln
∫
h(x) exp(η⊤T(x)) dx
(2.5)
notice in particular that it only depends on the natural parameters η.
We note that models with identical suﬃcient statistic functions make up a sub-family
of the exponential family with similar properties and the log-normalizer in turn follows
from a deﬁnition of suﬃcient statistics.
To get an intuition for how this family distributes probability mass, let us disregard
the base measure (which is often constant) and the log-normalizer which is constant for
some choice of η. this leaves us with the kernel exp(η⊤T(x)). Note that we take an inner
product between two vector quantities, which is a form of similarity measure. Speciﬁcally,
it will have a high value if the vectors share direction and if either or both vectors have
a high norm. The former implies a weighting of the suﬃcient statistics, while the latter
implies that high values for the parameters will induce more concentrated distributions.
2.2.1
Maximum Entropy
The exponential family can actually be derived from ﬁrst principles, as the solution to
the following problem [56]. Let us imagine you have observed some average statistics
of your data {xi}N
i=1 in the sense
1
N
∑N
i=1 T(xi) = α. We want to pick a distribution p
which has the same expected behavior, i.e.
Ep[T(x)] = α
(2.6)
Numerous distributions ﬁt these constraints, so we impose a further restriction: it has
to be the distribution with the highest possible (diﬀerential) entropy:
H(p) = −
∫
p(x) log p(x) dx.
(2.7)
The entropy is an important quantity in information theory. It is sometimes informally
referred to as uncertainty and in the continuous setting it is a measure of concentration.
Distributions with high entropy are ﬂat, with the highest entropy distribution on a ﬁnite
interval being the uniform distribution. Conversely, the distributions with the lowest
entropy (uncertainty) are highly concentrated distributions where a given sample will
fall in a narrow interval with high probability.
The entropy is a functional, that is, a function of functions, so formally we will have to
use the calculus of variations to solve it. The calculus of variations states, in its simplest
form, that the derivative of the integrand should be 0 on the entire space for the function

Note that since the base measure is often uniformly 1 or constant it often disappears or
adds a passive constant.

2.2.2
Information Geometry
Information geometry deals with spaces of distribution functions. We will need to know
two central quantities from this ﬁeld later, namely the score function and the Fisher
information matrix.
The score function V measures the sensitivity of the log-likelihood function to changes
in the parameter, by way of the gradient.
Deﬁnition 2.2.2 (Score). For a distribution p(x; η) we deﬁne a corresponding score
function V(η, x) as
V(η, x) = ∇η log p(x; η)
(2.12)
For an exponential family model, the score can be partially evaluated as below.
V(η, x) = ∇η log p(x; η) = T(x) −∇ηA(η)
(2.13)
The score function has the universal2 property that it has expectation 0 [56], following
E[V(η, x)|η] =
∫

∫

dx =
(2.14)

∫

(2.15)

information matrix, which is deﬁned as the second moment of the score.
Deﬁnition 2.2.3 (Fisher Information Matrix). For a parametric distribution p(x; η)
the Fisher information matrix is the second moment of the score function V(η, x)

2.2 The Exponential Family
9
A useful property of the exponential family is that the cumulants of the suﬃcient
statistics can be found as the derivatives of the log-normalizer [56]. As the cumulants
are related to the moments, we will show how to recover the mean and covariance from
the log-normalizer.
Lemma 2.2.4. The gradient of the log-normalizer is equal to the expectation of the
suﬃcient statistic.

h(x) exp(η⊤T(x))T(x) dx = E[T(x)].
(2.21)
Note that Lemma 2.2.4 allows us to specify the score of an exponential family distri-
bution in equation (2.13) a bit diﬀerently
V(η, x) = T(x) −E[T(x)]
(2.22)
I.e. it is the centered suﬃcient statistic.
To help us ﬁnd the covariance, we ﬁnd it convenient to establish the following lemma
[48]
Lemma 2.2.5. For a family of distributions q(x; ϕ) which are continuous in the param-
eter ϕ, and have a continuous gradient ∇ϕq(x; ϕ), the expectation obeys
∇ϕ Eq[h(x, ϕ)] = Eq[V(x, ϕ)h(x, ϕ) + ∇ϕh(x, ϕ)]
(2.23)
where V(x, ϕ) = ∇log q(x; ϕ) is the score function and h(x, ϕ) is an arbitrary continuous
function.
Proof. Under the continuity conditions, we can use Leibniz’ integral rule to exchange
the gradient and the integral operator,
∇ϕ E[h(x, ϕ)] = ∇ϕ
∫
q(x; ϕ)h(x, ϕ) dx =
∫
[∇ϕq(x; ϕ)] h(x, ϕ) dϕ +
∫
q(x; ϕ) [∇ϕh(x, ϕ)] dϕ =
∫
q(x; ϕ) ([∇ϕ ln q(x; ϕ)] h(x, ϕ) + ∇ϕh(x, ϕ)) dϕ
where we have used that ∇ln f(x) = ∇f(x)/f(x).
Where equation (2.23) simpliﬁes further if the function does not depend on ϕ.
==PAGE:18==
10
2 Preliminaries
Corollary 2.2.6. If the conditions of lemma 2.2.5 are met, and the function h(x) is
independent of ϕ, then
∇ϕ Eq[h(x)] = Eq[V(x, ϕ)h(x)]
This brings us to the following theorem.
Theorem 2.2.7. For an exponential family distribution p(x; η) the Hessian of the log-
normalizer is the Fisher information matrix.
∇2A(η) = E
[
(T(x) −E[T(x)])2]
= Cov(T(x), T(x)) = I(η)
(2.24)
Proof. We use Lemma (2.2.4) to remove the innermost gradient operator:
∇2A(η) = ∇E[T(x)]
The identity of equation (2.23) then simpliﬁes the expression to
∇2A(η) = E[V(η, x)T(x)] = E
[
T(x)T(x)⊤]
−E[T] E[T]⊤
which is exactly the covariance Cov(T(x), T(x)).
From the deﬁnition of the Fisher
information matrix in deﬁnition 2.16 we can complete the proof:
I(η) = Cov(T(x) −∇A(η), Cov((T) −∇A(η))) = Cov(T(x), T(x))
(2.25)
As the covariance is bilinear and all functions have covariance 0 with a constant.
This theorem makes it easier to ﬁnd the Fisher information matrix when working
with exponential families.
2.2.3
Expectation Parameter
For an exponential family distribution p(x; η) with natural parameter η, the expectation
parameter µ is given by
µ = Ep(x;η)[T(x)] .
(2.26)
From section 2.2.2 we know that this quantity is identiﬁable through the mapping
µ = ∇A(η), but the expectation parameter has an even tighter connection to the log-
normalizer and the natural parameter, by being its convex conjugate.
Convex conjugacy [56, 55], also known as the Legendre-Fenchel transform, is rooted
in the observation that the gradient of a convex function is unique in every point, since
the curvature is everywhere positive-semideﬁnite. So we can deﬁne a related function,
the convex conjugate, which is parametrized in the gradient slopes µ where there is a
one-to-one correspondence between the natural parameters η and the slopes µ.
For a convex function, e.g. the log-normalizer A(η), the convex conjugate is deﬁned
as:
A∗(µ) = sup
η (⟨µ, η⟩−A(η))
(2.27)
==PAGE:19==
2.2 The Exponential Family
11
Let us consider the tangent at some point η∗, which is incident at A(η∗)
A(η∗) = ⟨µ, η⟩+ b
(2.28)
where b is the intercept. An interesting property of b is that if we decrease it, the tangent
will be non-intersecting with the function, and if we increase it it will become a chord.
This is again a consequence of the function’s convexity. So for a particular µ, we want
to identify the smallest intercept that leads to an intersecting line. This can be stated
as the optimization problem:
inf (b) = inf [A(η) −⟨µ, η⟩] ⇔
(2.29)
A∗(µ) = sup (−b) = sup
η [⟨µ, η⟩−A(η)]
(2.30)
Which is the convex conjugate.
It’s then clear that it is a function of the tangent
hyperplane’s intercept, given its slope.
The convex conjugate can be deﬁned for any function, and it has the property that
it is always convex. For convex functions, it has the added beneﬁt of being reversible,
i.e. we can equivalently state
A(η) = sup
µ [⟨η, µ⟩−A∗(µ)] .
(2.31)
Which makes the duality between the functions more explicit. We can solve the internal
optimization problem in equation (2.31) by taking the derivative and setting it equal to
0, which gives us that µ should be selected such that η = ∇A∗(µ), and equivalently
µ = ∇A(η). This is consistent with the result from the previous section, and gives an
explicit relationship between η and µ. While this might all appear a bit academic, it
turns out we can exploit this feature later when we consider how to calculate natural
gradients.
2.2.4
Conjugate Prior
Conjugate priors are a particular feature of exponential families which, again, make
them eminently suited for Bayesian learning. Let us return to Bayes’ theorem which
states that a posterior is proportional to the prior times the likelihood
p(η|x, λ) ∝
N
∏
i=1
p(xi|η)p(x|λ)
(2.32)
If the prior is the conjugate prior of the likelihood, what we can state is that the posterior
is going to be of the same functional form as the prior. For an exponential family, this
implies that they will share suﬃcient statistics.
Note that due to the proportionality, all independent factors containing x or λ can
be subsumed in the normalization constant. This means we can reduce the right-hand
side product to
hprior(η) exp(η⊤Tllk(x) + λ⊤Tprior(η) −Allk(η))
==PAGE:20==
12
2 Preliminaries
To make this match the functional form of the prior, we need to ﬁnd a set of suﬃcient
statistics that assimilates the stray log-normalizer and makes the two inner products
match up. To accomplish this, we break λ into a scale ν and a shape χ parameter.
To deal with the log-normalizer, we deﬁne a scalar suﬃcient statistic weighted by ν to
be Tν(η) = −Allk(η) which makes the stray term add to an existing suﬃcient statistic
yielding a weight of ν + 1. Getting the two inner products to match up is then easily
accomplished by deﬁning a set of suﬃcient statistics weighted by χ which simply return
the input Tχ(η) = η. Then the posterior simpliﬁes to
hprior(η) exp(η⊤(Tllk(x) + χ) −(ν + 1)Allk(η))
(2.33)
Conjugate priors are useful as they ensure that we are staying in the well-known
exponential families, which means that we have a lot of formulas and properties in
closed-form.
To ﬁnish oﬀthis section we will describe the distributions that we will use later in
the thesis. The four distributions we consider here happen to make up two conjugate
pairs, as the beta distribution is the conjugate prior of the Bernoulli and the Dirichlet
is the conjugate prior of the multinomial.
2.2.5
Distributions in the Family
Most well-known distributions reside in the exponential family; the Gaussian, Bernoulli,
Laplace, Dirichlet and Beta distributions are all examples of exponential family distribu-
tions. Only a few distributions in common use fall outside the box, namely the uniform
distribution on arbitrary intervals, the multinomial with an unknown number of sam-
ples, and a number of marginal distributions. We will be using four distributions in this
thesis, namely the Bernoulli, the Beta, the Dirichlet, and the single-sample multinomial,
which we will consider in more depth.
Bernoulli Distribution
The Bernoulli distribution is, with the exception of the uniform distribution and the
Dirac delta, probably the simplest distribution in common use. The analogy of choice
when describing the Bernoulli is that it models the probability of a biased coin-ﬂip;
the Bernoulli-distributed binary random variable X comes up heads (X = 1) with
probability θ and comes up tails (X = 0) with probability 1 −θ.
Ber(X = x; θ) = θx(1 −θ)1−x
(2.34)
The density function in equation (2.34) switches between the two probabilities depending
on the value of the variable.
The Bernoulli can easily be transformed to standard exponential family form by
applying exp(log(·)),
exp(x log(θ) + (1 −x) log(1 −θ)) = exp
(
x log
(

)
(2.35)

2.3
Optimization
Optimization is the mathematical discipline concerned with ﬁnding extreme points of
functions. In short, given an objective function f : RN →R we are concerned with
ﬁnding points x∗∈RN such that the function f takes its maximum value. For continuous
objective functions, suﬃcient conditions for a maximum point x∗are [42]:
∇f(x∗) = 0,
∇2f(x∗) ≤0.
(2.47)
That is, the gradient of the point should be 0 and the Hessian should be negative semi-
deﬁnite, denoted by the ≤relation, but deﬁned as the property that p⊤∇2f(x∗)p ≤0.
Together these conditions imply that no local change is better or worse and that if
we move away from the point the gradients will start to curve downwards implying a
decrease.
Sometimes, the stationary points can be found analytically, but often we have to
consider local numerical algorithms. These approaches resort to Taylor’s Theorem to
better understand the local behaviour of the function [42]:
Theorem 2.3.1 (Taylor’s Theorem). Suppose that f : RN →R is twice continuously
diﬀerentiable and that p ∈RN. Then we have that
f(x + p) = f(x) + ∇f(x + tp)⊤p
(1st order)
and
f(x + p) = f(x) + ∇f(x)⊤p + 1
2p⊤∇2f(x + tp)p
(2nd order)
for some t ∈(0, 1).
==PAGE:24==
16
2 Preliminaries
Using the 2nd order version of Taylor’s Theorem, we can consider the scaled direction
αp and then take the derivative with respect to α (i.e. the change as we move along the
direction p) to recover the expression
∇f(x)⊤p + αp⊤∇2f(x + tp)p
If we evaluate the gradient at α = 0, so at x, we retrieve the rate of descent ∇f(x)⊤p =
∥∇f∥∥p∥cos(θ) where θ is the “angle” between the vectors. If we constrain ∥p∥= 1
without loss of of generality, the maximal rate of increase corresponds to cos(θ) = 1 ⇒
p = ∇f(x)/∥∇f(x)∥which is exactly the gradient direction.
Algorithm 1 Gradient Ascent
1: Pick random starting point x0, set i ←0
2: repeat
3:
Calculate gradient direction p = ∇f
4:
choose appropriate step length α
5:
xi+1 ←xi + αp
6: until Convergence
Gradient ascent is one of the simplest optimization algorithms available and consists
of simply following the gradient direction from point to point. A key component here
is the step length α. As soon as we move away from x the curvature of the objective
function is going to come into play, meaning the ascent direction might change and the
direction we are pursuing might even change to be a descent direction if we step too far.
The direction is guaranteed to be an ascent direction for suﬃciently small steps, but we
also want to take as large steps as possible to achieve fastest possible convergence. We
will consider diﬀerent procedures for picking out these conditions.
2.3.1
Stochastic Gradient Ascent
While gradient ascent is robust, it scales poorly. To compute the gradient accurately you
need to visit all the data points each iteration to determine their individual contributions.
Stochastic gradient descent3 (SGD) algorithms provide a more scalable alternative.
Instead of computing the gradient, we make a noisy estimate of it and follow that. As
long as we are walking in the right direction on average, it can be shown that we’ll
converge towards a stationary point.
This idea was pioneered by Robbins and Monro in [46] under the name of stochastic
approximation, which is a slightly more general framework. Their approach later came
to be known as the Robbins-Monro algorithm [6].
In stochastic approximation we want to ﬁnd the root of a function M(x), i.e. M(x∗) =
0 but we only observe noisy values y of M, with the property that E[y | x] = M(x). In
our case, we might want to consider the gradient of a loss function M(ϕ) = ∇L(ϕ) over
some parameters ϕ, so that the root corresponds to a stationary point.
3to follow the original papers more closely we will use equivalent descent notation for this section
==PAGE:25==
2.3 Optimization
17
Algorithm 2 The Robbins-Monro algorithm
Initialize x0, set n ←0
repeat
Sample noisy gradient gn
Update xn+1 ←xn −angi
until Convergence
Algorithm 2 reveals the connections to gradient descent, making the reasoning behind
calling it stochastic gradient ascent more obvious.
A partial argument for convergence follows. Convergence of the algorithm occurs if
the variance sequence bn = E[(xn −x∗)2] converges to 0. To get an idea of when this

2 Preliminaries
2.3.2
Learning Rates and Line Search
After a search direction p has been picked out, we can deﬁne an implicit one-dimensional
function along said direction
φ(α) = f(x + αp)
(2.52)
Finding a suitable, or even optimal, α is an integral part of a gradient descent procedure
and is solved by applying a line search procedure.
Since spending time searching for the optimal step length along an intermediate
search direction is wasteful, we relax our demands a bit and look for an α fulﬁlling the
so-called Wolfe conditions.
Deﬁnition 2.3.3 (Strong Wolfe Conditions). The strong Wolfe conditions hold if both
of the following inequalities hold
f(x + αp) ≥f(x) + c1α∇f(x)⊤p
(suﬃcient increase condition)
|∇f(x + αp)⊤p| ≤c2|∇f(x)⊤p|
(curvature condition)
with 0 < c1 < c2 < 1.
The suﬃcient increase condition (sometimes called the Armijo condition) is there to
safeguard us against taking steps so long that the gradient is no longer a good measure
for the expected increase. The curvature condition on the other hand guarantees that
we are moving closer to a stationary point: the gradient has to be “ﬂatter” at the new
point. This also pushes the line search method towards taking longer more signiﬁcant
steps. These conditions are generally suﬃcient to guarantee convergence for a number
of common models [42].
We will consider two common algorithms for solving the line search problem: one
simple algorithm, and one which is a bit more convoluted.
The ﬁrst algorithm is known as the backtracking algorithm. It is primarily used
when it is expensive to evaluate the gradient. Backtracking only guarantees a step size
that observes the suﬃcient decrease condition, but uses a heuristic to pick a step size
which is likely to observe the curvature condition as well.
Algorithm 3 The backtracking line search algorithm
1: choose initial step α and contraction factor ρ ∈(0, 1)
2: repeat
3:
α ←ρα
4: until Suﬃcient increase condition holds
If we set the initial α to be a “large” step and the contraction factor ρ is not too
small, the ﬁnal step size is likely to be quite a long step which should make it more likely
that the curvature condition holds. Although based on a simple heuristic, this algorithm
often works quite well [42].
==PAGE:27==
2.3 Optimization
19
We will call the second algorithm Wolfe search. It appears in Nocedal and Wright
[42, page 60] and appears to be cobbled together from a number of papers including
Davidon’s original paper on BFGS (another popular optimization algorithm) [13].
The algorithm ﬁrst coarsely reﬁnes an interval [αi−1, αi] and then applies a recursive
interpolation procedure to search
Algorithm 4 The Wolfe search algorithm
1: Propose α1 ∈(0, αmax) and set i ←1
2: loop
3:
if suﬃcient increase does not hold for αi then
4:
Search in [αi−1, αi]
5:
end if
6:
if Curvature conditions holds for αi then
7:
return αi
8:
end if
9:
if αi has decreasing gradient then
10:
Search in [αi−1, αi]
11:
end if
12:
Propose new αi+1 and set i ←i + 1
13: end loop
The idea is that for the interval [αlo, αhi] we know that αlo observes the suﬃcient
decrease condition and that αhi bounds our search interval from above with a step size
which is insuﬃcient in some way.
If an interval search procedure is executed, then αhi is a step too far by virtue of
failing either the suﬃcient increase or decreasing gradient test. In an interval search, a
new proposal α ∈[αlo, αhi] is proposed and returned if it observes the Wolfe conditions.
If it is not Wolfe-consistent we recursively search a new interval:
1. if α failed the suﬃcient increase condition the step was too far and we investigate
the smaller interval [αlo, α]
2. if it failed the Wolfe condition but not the suﬃcient increase condition, we can
take a longer step and set the new interval to [α, αhi]
The missing piece is how to ﬁnd a good step size within a proposed interval. We are
going to use a scheme which is occasionally known as Davidon’s cubic interpolation
scheme and shows up in a few places in various versions [13, 42].
Recall the deﬁnition of the line search objective function ϕ(α) = f(x + αp). The
scheme then assumes we have access to y0 = ϕ(αlo), y1 = ϕ(αhi), g0 = ∇ϕ(αlo), and
g1 = ∇ϕ(αhi). We furthermore make the assumption that ∇ϕ(αlo) > 0, i.e. it is an
ascent direction. This can be made to hold for the Wolfe search algorithm. We then try
to ﬁt a cubic polynomial
ψ(s) = a + bs + cs2 + ds3,
∇ψ(s) = b + 2cs + 3ds2
(2.53)
==PAGE:28==
20
2 Preliminaries
where the trick is to assume that ψ(0) = y0 and ψ(β) = y1 where β = αhi −αlo. This
simpliﬁes everything considerably.
When s = 0 the cubic function reduces to constant terms so we get that a = y0 and
b = g0 for free. We can use that ψ(β) = y1 to solve for c
c = y1 −y0 −g0β −dβ3
β2
= y1 −y0
β2
−g0
β −dβ
We can then solve for d to procure
g1 = g0 + 2β
(y1 −y0
β2
−g0

Adaptive Step Size Schemes
For stochastic algorithms such as Robbins-Monro, line search will do little good as we
are not even sure we have picked a descent direction and occasionally we might not even
be able to (or willing to) provide information about our progress. Yet choosing a poor or
naive step size scheme, such as the annealing scheme we presented with Robbins-Munro,
can lead to sub-par results.
Adaptive step size schemes [50, 58, 15] have been proposed as an alternative and
have seen wide adaptation, particularly in the deep learning community.
Duchi et al. gained considerable traction with their AdaGrad (adaptive-gradient)
algorithm [15], despite only dedicating a couple of paragraphs to the particular version
that came to see wide application and which is frequently used as a benchmark for other
adaptive schemes. Their idea is quite simple and deﬁnes the learning rate ηt as
ηn =
η0
√∑n
t=0 ˆg2
t
(2.56)
η0 is a global learning rate while ˆgt is the (sampled) gradient at iteration t. The learning
rate is deﬁned individually for each coordinate direction. The eﬀect is that directions
which see a lot of activity are damped considerably, while directions which are only
rarely active are annealed slowly. This “normalizes” the diﬀerent coordinate directions
with respect to each other, ensuring that learning proceeds evenly in all directions. This
is similar to the eﬀect of preconditioning with an inverse Hessian where each direction
is scaled by how quickly the gradient changes along that direction (the curvature).
One potential issue with AdaGrad is the monotone decrease of the learning rate
as the gradient accumulates in the denominator, which makes the method less likely to
respond to changes in curvature [58, 50].
As a potential solution to this issue, Zeiler presented an extension called AdaDelta
[58]. His extension comes in two parts. First, to deal with the monotone behavior he
turns the global average into an exponentially-weighted window:
RMS(ˆg)t = (1 −ρ) RMS(ˆg)t−1 + ρˆg2
t
(2.57)
The t index indicates the value of the accumulator in the t’th iteration, making this a
recursive formula which we initialize with 0. The free parameter ρ ∈(0, 1) is a weighting
parameter which ensures that the accumulated gradient will “forget” the previous state
and adapt to changes in the curvature.
==PAGE:30==
22
2 Preliminaries
The second modiﬁcation is based on a number of heuristic arguments, but has shown
to be quite a powerful extension. Zeiler argues that there is a “mismatch of units” in
AdaGrad. In an analogy to physics, let us say that m is the unit of our function f(x)
and the parameter x is measured in units s. Then the gradient has units m/s and the
curvature has units m/s2. If the gradient update x + ∆x for is to have the correct
units, ∆x should have units s. For the Newton step, this holds as H−1 has units s2/m
which cancels out with the units m/s of the gradient in the step ∆x = H−1. AdaGrad
has units m/s in the denominator which cancels out with the gradient when multiplied
together, leaving it “dimensionless”. This leads Zeiler to propose multiplying by ∆x
which has the correct units s. This derivation can be made slightly more formal by
considering it as an approximation of the Hessian:
∆x = [∇2f(x)]−1∇f(x) ⇔[∇2f(x)]−1 =
∆x
∇f(x)
(2.58)
This odd heuristic idea leads to the formula for AdaDelta.
ηt =
√
RMS(ηˆg)t−1 + ε
√
RMS(ˆg)t + ε
(2.59)
ε is a regularization term and serves as a default step size for the ﬁrst iteration. The
numerator will be large proportional to the denominator when the step sizes are large,
which in turn occurs for consistently small gradients. So where AdaGrad shrinks large
gradients until they are on the same scale as the smallest gradient, AdaDelta also scales
small gradients up. note that the numerator lags one step behind the denominator. One
useful eﬀect of this is as a safeguard against sudden large gradients: if a large gradient
occurs, it is going to accumulate in the denominator and dampen itself without adversely
inﬂuencing the numerator.
2.4
Nonparametric Distributions
Nonparametric distributions are a particular class of very ﬂexible distributions which
allow them to adapt to the size and form of the data. That the distributions are non-
parametric does not mean that they do not possess parameters, but rather that they are
not deﬁned by any ﬁnite ﬁxed set of parameters.
2.4.1
Exchangeability
Exchangeability is a core concept in nonparametrics [28], and in Bayesian statistics in
general, although perhaps a bit less explicitly [16].
In probabilistic modeling we are often faced with a sequence of random samples from
some unknown generative process. As part of our modeling, we need to decide whether
the order matters - does X1 follow the same distribution as XN? Or, perhaps more
pertinently, are the random variables dependent on each other?
Exchangeability for a sequence of random draws is a convenient assumption which
states that the order does not matter. Or, more formally
==PAGE:31==
2.4 Nonparametric Distributions
23
Deﬁnition 2.4.1 ((Inﬁnitely) Exchangeable Sequence). An (inﬁnite) sequence of ran-
dom variables Xi is exchangeable if
p(X1, X2...) = p(Xσ(1), Xσ(2)...)
(2.60)
for any permutation σ(i).
Many Bayesian model have a set of variables which are i.i.d. (independently and
identically distributed), and it turns out this is just a hidden assumption of exchange-
ability: if random variables are i.i.d. i.e. p({Xi}) = ∏N
i=1 p(Xi) then exchangeability
holds by simple commutativity of the factors. The i.i.d. claim is stronger than exchange-
ability though, as it suﬃces for the variables to be conditionally independent. Imagine
that in the i.i.d. case that the distribution is itself a random variable Θ, drawn from
a random measure (a probability distribution over distributions). Then the generative
process Θ ∼ν,
Xi ∼Θ retains the exchangeability property. This is really the tradi-
tional setup for any Bayesian model: the prior (ν) determines a distribution on a set of
parametric distributions (Θ) from which the samples are drawn.
The exchangeability property is further preserved if we consider the samples to be
drawn from a mixture of several draws from Θi ∼ν. This is where it gets interesting,
as it brings us to a representation theorem for all exchangeable distributions [43]:
Theorem 2.4.2 (de Finetti’s Theorem). If a probability distribution p(X) is inﬁnitely
exchangeable it must hold that
p({Xi}∞
i=1) =
∫
M
∞
∏
i=1
θ(Xi)ν(dθ)
(2.61)
where X is a random variable taking values in the domain X, θ is a realization of the
random probability measure Θ taking values in the space M of all measures on X, and
ν is a probability measure on the space of distributions M.
While this result might appear a bit obtuse at ﬁrst, it is stating (almost) the same
thing we just did: that a mixture of independent distributions is exchangeable. Only
it goes the other way as well, stating that all exchangeable distributions must have a
representation as such a mixture. This is convenient as it means that no matter how
complex a distribution is, it can be reduces to a mixture of simple objects if it has the
simple property of exchangeability.
Another way to look at it is as a two-stage procedure: ﬁrst, you sample a measure
Θ ∼ν and then you sample the sequence Xi ∼Θ. The joint probability distribution is
simply the marginal distribution of X after Θ has been marginalized out.
The concept of exchangeability can be extended by generalizing the notion of or-
der invariance to invariance under some particular set of permutations on the random
variables. This in turn allows us to consider exchangeability in the context of random
structures other than sequences such as partitions, graphs, and arrays.
We will follow Orbanz and Roy [43] in formulating the general form for exchange-
ability results.
They consider a space of inﬁnite structures X∞(be they sequences,
==PAGE:32==
24
2 Preliminaries
partitions, graphs, etc) and the case where we have a family of permutations Σ so that
if a distribution is exchangeable with respect to the family it has to observe the identity
p(σ(x∞)) = p(x∞) for σ ∈Σ and x∞∈X∞.
A representation theorem for such a notion of exchangeability is constructed around a
parameter space Θ which deﬁnes a set of probabilistic models called the ergodic measures
{pθ, θ ∈Θ} ⊂M(X∞) where M(X∞) is the set of all distributions on the set of structures
X∞.
An ideal set of ergodic measures should consist of a small subset of all possible
exchangeable distributions, and they should be simple. This bears relation to Choquet
Theory which states that all points in a convex set can be represented as averages of its
extreme points. It is in some sense the same thing we are trying to achieve here.
With a set of ergodic measures in place, a Finetti-style representation theorem states
that, for some mixing measure ν over Θ,
p(X∞) =
∫
Θ
pθ(X∞)ν(dθ)
(2.62)
These representation theorems are typical, but it is by no means a universal result. It
has to be derived for each type of structure and each notion of exchangeability. We will
later see that it can sometimes be convenient to use a diﬀerent, although fundamentally
similar, representation.
2.4.2
Partitions and the Chinese Restaurant Process
We will consider the idea of distributions over partitions, where a partition is deﬁned as
follows.
Deﬁnition 2.4.3. A partition Π = {Si}N
i=1 is a set of subsets Si ⊂Ωsuch that ∪N
i=1Si =
Ωand ∀ijSi ∩Sj = ∅.
Partitions are intrinsically involved in clustering and will be necessary later when we
consider block models. In particular, the Chinese Restaurant Process will turn out to
be an important piece.
The Chinese restaurant process (CRP) has many equivalent formulations, but we
will start with the one which is easiest to understand as it can be explained through a
simple analogy.
Let us imagine a restaurant with inﬁnitely many tables. A single person is seated
at the ﬁrst table. When a new person arrives, he can either seat himself at one of the
already occupied tables or he can seat himself at a new table. The preference for sitting
at an occupied table i is proportional to the number of people already seated at it (ni),
while the preference for sitting at an unoccupied table is proportional to a parameter α.
If we now assume that the customer picks the table randomly based on his preference,
we have deﬁned a stochastic process.
Deﬁnition 2.4.4 (Chinese Restaurant Process). The CRP deﬁnes an inﬁnite sequence
of random variables Xi ∈N. Xi = 1 with probability 1, while the rest are distributed

the distribution over random variables is likewise exchangeable.
So if the distribution is exchangeable, we can look for an underlying distribution in
terms of a mixture of ergodic measures. We do not require a new representation theorem
since representing the partition as indices allows us to piggyback on de Finetti’s theorem.

2.4.3
The Dirichlet Process
The CRP becomes interesting when we consider it as a prior for more conventional
models. The CRP mixture model takes the general form
{zn}N
n=1 ∼CRP(α)
θk ∼G0,
for k = 1, 2, ...
xn | zn ∼F(θzn)
So we can use the CRP to split the data into an arbitrary partition and then deﬁne
a generative distribution on each partition set (each table, in the restaurant analogy)
deﬁned by some parameter vector θ. This hierarchical description is easy to relate to,
but it will be advantageous to consider a related model called the Blackwell-MacQueen
urn (BM urn) [28]. Urn models again build on analogy; this time we are asked to draw
colored balls out of an urn. In the BM urn model, we start with a single special “black”
ball. Anytime we draw the black ball, we put the black ball back and add a new ball
with a new random color θ ∼G0. If we draw a colored ball with color θk, we immediately
put it back and add another ball of the same color. The model can be described in a
compact manner as
θn+1|θ1, ..., θn ∼
α
N + αG0 +
1
N + α
n
∑
k=1
δθk
(BM urn)
The BM urn consists of an arbitrary measure G0 and an atomic measure ∑δθ which
places mass on individual points. By the analogy, it should be somewhat transparent
that the two ﬁrst steps of the CRP mixture and the BM urn are equivalent. The main
diﬀerence is that in the CRP we assign customers to tables, and then draw a parameter
vector θ for each table, while in the BM urn we draw the parameter vector directly.
The atomic measure in the BM formulation is a necessary part for any distribution
aﬃliated with clustering. If the measure has no atomic components (it is diﬀuse) the
probability of drawing the same parameter vector twice is 0; only by placing mass on
discrete atomic points can we ensure clustering behaviour [28].
Through its connection to the CRP, the BM urn is an exchangeable process and de
Finetti’s theorem therefore says there must be an underlying random measure.
This measure must be atomic since we can draw the same vector multiple times, and
should therefore be of the form
µ =

(2.66)
where ∑wk = 1 to normalize the measure. It turns out that the underlying random
measure of the BM urn (and thus the CRP by proxy) is the measure with weights deﬁned

2.4 Nonparametric Distributions
27
This process is known as the Dirichlet process [28] and this particular way of expressing
it is known as Sethuraman’s stick-breaking construction [52]. The intuition is that we
have a “stick” of length 1, representing our available probability mass. Since a beta
sample lies in the open interval (0, 1), we can break of a small piece of length β1 and
place that mass at θ1. This leaves us with a stick of length 1 −β1 of which we then
break oﬀa fraction β2, and so on.
2.4.4
Exchangeable Arrays
The notion of sequential exchangeability does not generalize readily to arrays of random
variables - if we allowed every possible reordering of the array elements the structure of
the array becomes meaningless. Instead, we deﬁne the notion of joint exchangeability
Deﬁnition 2.4.6 (Joint Exchangeability). An (inﬁnite) random 2-dimensional array
Xij is jointly exchangeable if
p(Xij) = p(Xπ(i)π(j))
(2.68)
for every permutation π of N.
A representation theorem exists for jointly exchangeable random arrays as well, called
the Aldous-Hoover representation theorem [43].
Theorem 2.4.7 (Aldous-Hoover). A random array (Xij) taking values in X is jointly
exchangeable if and only if there exists a random function F : [0, 1]3 →X
p(Xij) = p(F(Ui, Uj, U{i,j}))
(2.69)
where {Ui}i∈N and {U{i,j}}i,j∈N are uniform variables in the [0, 1] interval.
This theorem is quite a bit diﬀerent from what we have seen so far, but the idea
is the same. To see this, recall the idea of inverse transform sampling [6]: a random
variable X can be sampled by sampling a uniform variable U and then mapping it to
the real sample space using the inverse CDF4 ψ−1. Returning to our two step sampling
interpretation of de Finetti’s theorem, sampling the random measure is equivalent to
sampling a random CDF, allowing us to (informally) rewrite de Finetti’s theorem as
(X1, X2, ...)
d= (F(U1), F(U2), ...)
This formulation is quite similar to that of the Aldous-Hoover theorem. Sampling the
random function F yields a function f : [0, 1]3 →X and, intuitively, Ui and Uj then
pick out a particular inverse CDF ˆψ−1
ij (X) ≡f(Ui, Uj, X) for the array element Xij. The
last uniform number U{i,j} then picks out a random number from this inverse CDF.
4While the CDF is monotone, plateaus can occur which means the ordinary inverse won’t exist.
Fortunately, there are ways around this [43].

CHAPTER 3
Variational Inference
As we saw in section 2.1.2, the core problem of probabilistic inference is how to evaluate
the posterior probability distribution,
p(θ|D) = p(D|θ)p(θ)
p(D)
=
p(D|θ)p(θ)
∫
p(D|θ)p(θ) dθ
(3.1)
which is often intractable to compute due to the presence of the integral.
Computing the integral by quadrature or other numerical methods is often hindered
by the curse of dimensionality, which has led to a number of other methods for calculating
the integral.
In Bayesian learning, there are two competing paradigms [6, 16].
Markov Chain Monte Carlo methods produce approximations of the exact poste-
rior. Black-box samplers are used to draw samples from the unknown posterior.
Predictions average over the sampled models.
Variational methods perform exact inference using approximate posteriors. Simpler
ﬁxed-form distributions are optimized to be as close to the true posterior as possi-
ble. Predictions integrate over the approximation.
Both paradigms have weaknesses and advantages. MCMC methods draw samples
randomly, slowly converging to the true posterior. In the limit, these samples will be
samples from the exact posterior, but it is diﬃcult to tell whether it has converged. The
algorithms often also suﬀer from being slow and scaling poorly. Variational methods
are faster, but work with simpler approximations to the posterior, at the risk of losing
information. They reduce the inference problem to maximization of a non-convex objec-
tive, which can be optimized by conventional optimization algorithms. This problem is
solvable and it is easy to determine convergence, but since the objective is non-convex
global optimization is intractable, and the solution we converge to is (without more
knowledge about the objective) likely to be a local, but not global, maximum.
We will only deal with variational algorithms in this thesis, although Gibbs sampling
will be mentioned in passing.
3.1
Variational Inference
Variational inference is named after the Calculus of Variations, which is concerned with
the optimization of functionals [56].
A functional maps a function to the real line.
We have already seen examples of functionals, such as the diﬀerential entropy, and we
==PAGE:37==
3.1 Variational Inference
29
have even employed basic variational methods in proving the functional form of the
exponential family as maximum entropy distributions.
Variational inference spans over a number of diﬀerent algorithms, but what they
all have in common is that they replace the true posterior with an approximate one
q(θ) ≈p(θ|D), by minimizing a distance measure (ostensibly a functional) between the
two distributions.
3.1.1
Statistical Distance
The concept of a distance between two distributions leaves a lot of room for interpre-
tation. Is a distribution close to another when the curves of the density functions are
alike, or when samples drawn from them are similar? Is it more important to be close
in intervals of high probability or to match the support?
A naive (yet useful) distance measure is the total variation distance, which is simply
the area between the distributions:
DTV (p(θ), q(θ)) = 1
2
∫
|p(θ) −q(θ)| dθ
(3.2)
From an intuitive point of view, the issue with the total variational distance is that it
is impartial to where the diﬀerence in probability mass takes place. If the same amount
of probability mass is taken from the tail vis-à-vis the mode of a distribution, then it is
going to make a rare event even more unlikely in the former case while in the latter case
it the lost mass will have a smaller impact.
To better account for this aspect, we will consider a more ﬂexible class of distance
measures between probability distributions colloquially known as divergences [36]. Di-
vergences are weaker than metrics as they can be asymmetric, but they still observe
non-negativity and are only identically 0 when the probability distributions are exactly
the same. The Kullback-Leibler distribution is the most common such divergence and
is deﬁned as[56, 33]
KL(p∥q) = H×(p, q) −H(p) = Ep
[
ln p(x)
q(x)
]
=
∫
p(x) ln p(x)
q(x) dx
(3.3)
The function H×(q, p) = −
∫
p(x) ln q(x) dx is denoted the cross-entropy. The Kullback-
Leibler divergence is tightly related to the ﬁeld of information theory where it is some-
times referred to as relative entropy. In information theory the quantity −ln p(x) is de-
noted the information of a random variable X being observed to be x, making entropy
the average information of a distribution. Under the logarithm a diﬀerence between
numbers close to 0 is more signiﬁcant than a diﬀerence between numbers close to 1, so
this already seems like a more sound measure for statistical divergence. We can prove
that the KL divergence is non-negative by applying Jensen’s inequality by using that
−ln(·) is a strictly convex function
KL(p∥q) = Ep
[
f
(q(x)
p(x)
)]
≥f
(∫
p(x)q(x)
p(x) dx
)
) = 0
(3.4)
==PAGE:38==
30
3 Variational Inference
That it is strictly convex implies that the inequality only becomes an equality if the
two distributions are identical almost everywhere [6]. This in turn implies that if we
average the information over any other distribution, as is the case with the cross-entropy,
the average information will be higher. This gap in information is characteristic of a
a diﬀerence between the distributions, and by taking the diﬀerence between the cross-
entropy and the entropy itself, we measure how much information is lost on average by
using the alternate distribution q.
As noted above, statistical divergences are often asymmetric, and this is also the
case for the Kullback-Leibler divergence. If the arguments are switched, the divergence
measures how badly the true posterior p ﬁts the approximation q instead of the other
way around. The minimum is still achieved when the distributions are exactly equal,
but the divergences put emphasis on diﬀerent aspects.
In [36] the divergences KL(p∥q) and KL(q∥p) were exempliﬁed as two members of
the α-divergence family, at opposite ends of the spectrum: one is at α = −1 while the
other is at α = 1. The sign of α determines whether the divergence is zero-forcing
or zero-avoiding. In the former case, the approximate distribution will force itself to
be 0 where the target distribution is 0. This implies it will emphasize the tails of the
distribution, usually by putting more of its mass on the mode. If it is 0-avoiding, on the
other hand, it will strive not to put mass in high probability regions. This will lead to
an emphasis on covering the entire distribution, disregarding particular modes [36].
Variational approximations based on zero-avoiding divergences have been shown to
lead to better prediction accuracy empirically [36]. Unfortunately, the zero-avoiding
KL KL(p∥q) divergence is intractable to optimize directly as it requires expectations
over the unknown posterior. While there does exist an approximate method for solving
the problem known as Expectation Propagation [6], we will settle for optimizing the
argument-swapped divergence KL(q∥p) for this thesis.
3.1.2
The Lower Bound
Minimizing divergences is complicated, even when working with the simpler argument-
swapped KL-divergence. To get around this problem, variational inference relies on the
following relationship between the KL divergence and the evidence term p(D)
Theorem 3.1.1. For a posterior p(θ|D) and an approximation q(θ) over the same
domain Θ, the following identity holds
ln p(D) = Eq[ln p(θ, D)] + H(q) + KL(q∥p)
(3.5)
where KL(·∥·) is the Kullback-Leibler divergence and H is the diﬀerential Shannon en-
tropy.
Proof. To prove the identity, we take our oﬀset in the Kullback-Leibler divergence be-

3.1 Variational Inference
31
tween the two distributions
KL(q∥p) =
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln
q(θ)
p(θ|D) dθ =
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln q(θ)p(D)
p(θ, D) dθ ⇔
(3.6)
KL(q∥p) =
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln q(θ) dθ +
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln p(D) dθ −
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln p(θ, D) dθ
(3.7)
The ﬁrst term of the right-hand side is the negative entropy, for the second the log-
evidence is constant and can be put outside and the remaining integrand integrates to
1, and the last term is the expectation of the joint. So by moving everything but the
log-evidence to the left-hand side we prove the identity.
Since we want to circumvent working with the KL-divergence we group the ﬁrst two
terms of equation (3.5) into a single functional denoted the evidence lower bound L(q)
(ELBO),
Deﬁnition 3.1.2 (Evidence Lower Bound). The evidence lower bound for a joint prob-
ability distribution p(θ, D) with ﬁxed data D is deﬁned as
L(q) = Eq[ln p(θ, D)] + H(q) =
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln p(θ, D) dθ −
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln q(θ) dθ.
(3.8)
where q(θ) is a distribution over the same parameter space as the posterior p(θ|D).
Since the log-evidence is a constant there is a dual relationship between L(q) and
the KL divergence: minimizing the KL divergence is equivalent to maximizing the lower
bound.
As the solution to the (functional) variational problem is trivially the posterior it-
self (as it uniquely minimizes the KL divergence), we will ﬁnd it necessary to restrict
the domain to something that will yield simpler tractable solutions. A simple way to
achieve this is to restrict the variational distribution q(θ; ϕ) to a parametric family of
distributions, parametrized by ϕ. When this is the case we will denote the bound by
L(ϕ) and it turns into a parametric, rather than a functional, objective.
3.1.3
Geometry of the ELBO
We will investigate the nature of the ELBO to get a better understanding of our objective
function. First, we should note that it can be rewritten in a slightly more familiar way
as
L(q) = −
∫
Θ
q(θ) ln
q(θ)
p(θ, D) dθ.
(3.9)
By comparing this to equation (3.3) we note that this is superﬁcially similar to a nega-
tive KL-divergence. The main diﬀerence is that p(θ, D) is not a legitimate probability
distribution, but is only proportional to one, namely the posterior p(θ|D) ∝p(θ, D). So
in some sense we have substituted the minimization of the true KL divergence for the
==PAGE:40==
32
3 Variational Inference
maximization of the unnormalized negative KL divergence, and the gap between these
two divergences is exactly the normalizing constant p(D).
To get to grips with the learning problem itself, we deﬁne the pointwise loss ℓ(θ, ϕ) =
ln p(θ, D) −ln q(θ; ϕ), where ϕ are the parameters controlling the shape of q. Given the
point-wise loss, the ELBO is given by L(q) = Eq[ℓ(θ)]. The derivative of the point-wise
loss in the variational parameters ϕ is easily computable as
∇ϕℓ(θ, ϕ) = −∇ln q(θ; ϕ) = −V(θ, ϕ)
(3.10)
where V(θ, ϕ) is the score function, deﬁned previously in deﬁnition 2.12.
This is interesting as it brings us into the domain of information geometry again. Let
us consider the gradient of the true objective L, then we can use lemma 2.2.5 to carry
the gradient into the expectation
Proposition 3.1.3 (Gradient of the ELBO). The gradient of the parametric ELBO
L(ϕ) in the parameters ϕ for a family of distributions q(θ; ϕ) is
∇L(ϕ) = Eq[V(θ, ϕ)ℓ(θ, ϕ)] ,
(3.11)
where V(θ, ϕ) is the score function.
Proof. The proposition follows rather straightforwardly from applying Lemma 2.2.5
∇L(ϕ) = ∇Eq[ℓ(ϕ)] = Eq[V(θ, ϕ)ℓ(θ, ϕ)] + Eq[∇ϕℓ(θ, ϕ)] = Eq[V(θ, ϕ)ℓ(θ, ϕ)] ,
(3.12)
where the last line follows from the fact that E[V] = 0 is always true.
As the gradient of the point-wise term is again easily calculable
∇ϕ[V(θ, ϕ)ℓ(θ, ϕ)] = ∇V(θ, ϕ)ℓ(θ, ϕ) −V(θ, ϕ)V(θ, ϕ)⊤
(3.13)
we can repeat the process almost to the letter to get the Hessian:
Proposition 3.1.4 (Hessian of the ELBO). The Hessian of the ELBO is deﬁned as
∇2L(ϕ) = Eq

One thing we should pay attention to is the convexity1, or lack of the same, of the
objective function. Convex optimization is more well-developed and more robust than
non-convex optimization, particularly because convex functions have at most one mode
as they have positive curvature everywhere [42]. Unfortunately, we can make no absolute
claims about the convexity of the Hessian we have detailed here as it depends heavily
on the sign of the gradient of the score function and the curvature of the distribution q.
3.1.4
Mean-Field Assumption
To maximize the lower bound, we need to consider some parametrization of q. The mean-
ﬁeld assumption simpliﬁes this considerably by assuming that the variational distribution
decomposes into independent distributions over the individual variables
q(θ) =
∏
qi(θi; ϕi)
(3.16)
where ϕi parametrizes qi.
The beneﬁt of mean-ﬁeld is that the expectation now distributes over products, and
that the entropy decomposes.
Using mean-ﬁeld, let us investigate how the bound depends on each variable. In
particular, let us consider the parameter θj, holding the rest ﬁxed. By using the chain
rule, we can decompose the ELBO as
L(q) =
∫
q(θ; ϕ) ln p(θj|θ\j, D) dθ +
∫
q\j(θ\j; ϕ\j) ln p(θ\j, D) dθ\j
−
∫
qj(θj; ϕj) ln q(θj; ϕj) dθj −
∑
i
∫
qi(θi; ϕi) ln q(θi; ϕi) dθi.
(3.17)
If we wanted to optimize with respect to ϕj only, terms without this parameter would
be constant, so we can leave them out. We will call the resulting function the ϕj-ELBO
L(ϕj), and it can be stated explicitly as
1For maximization it is actually concavity we are interested in, but we will conﬂate the two terms
to maintain consistency with the literature.
==PAGE:42==
34
3 Variational Inference
L(ϕj) =
∫
q(θ; ϕ) ln p(θj|θ\j, D) dθ −
∫
qj(θj) ln q(θj; ϕj) dθj ⇔
(3.18)
L(ϕj) = E
[
ln p(θj|θ\j, D)
]
−Eqj[ln qj(θj; ϕj)] .
(3.19)
The expectation E
[
ln p(θj|θ\j, D)
]
is conditioned on everything, but it is only actually
necessary to take the markov blanket of θj into account. The markov blanket is the set
of variables which makes θj conditionally independent of the rest of the variables. In
a Bayesian network the blanket is given by the variable’s children, its parents and its
co-parents [6]. Probability distributions over a random variable conditioned on their
full markov blanket is sometimes called a complete conditional and for notational conve-
nience we will occasionally denote them by p(θj|...). Note furthermore that the second
expectation term is quite simply the entropy of the marginal variational distribution qj,
so we can also state the ϕj-ELBO as
L(ϕj) = Eqj[E[ln p(ϕj|...) | ϕj]] + H(qj)
(3.20)
where we have made the inﬂuence of ϕj a bit more explicit by using iterated expectations.
Intuitively, to maximize this objective, we want to ﬁnd a distribution which simultane-
ously puts as much of its mass as possible near the mode of the “expected” conditional,
while at the same time being as diﬀuse and broad as possible to optimize the entropy.
Recall that this function was deﬁned to have the property ∇ϕjL(q) = ∇ϕjL(ϕj), since
the gradient would cancel out the constant terms. So, coordinate-wise, it suﬃces to op-
timize the expected value of the complete conditional and the entropy of the variational
marginal.

3.1.6
Variational Inference for the Exponential Family
We will make the assumption that the qj is an exponential family distribution parametrized
by ϕj. Furthermore, we will assume that it is of the same form as its respective complete
conditional p(θj|θ−j, D), parametrized by ηj. This implies that their base measure and
suﬃcient statistic functions are identical.

3.2
Natural Gradient
The natural gradient was pioneered by Amari et al. in [3, 4]. He argues that if our
parameters are not situated in an Euclidean space, then the steepest descent direction
is not necessarily given by the regular gradient.
Manifolds are a mathematical structure used to model irregular spaces that are only
locally similar to regular Euclidean space[4]. If the manifold is smooth, we can deﬁne
distances, areas, and angles on the manifold by introducing another mathematical object
called a metric tensor, which turns the combined structure into a Riemannian manifold.
While ostensibly a bilinear map g(u, v) between two tangent vectors it is common
to write a Riemannian metric as a matrix G with elements Gij(x) = g(ei(w), ej(w)) for
some basis {ei(w)}N
i=1. Note the dependence on w: since we are not in a standard linear
space, the basis and the metric can change with each inﬁnitesimal step.
The notion of distance is encapsulated in the choice of metric, and is given by [4, 20]
Dw0(δw) =
√
δw⊤G(w)δw
(3.28)
where δw is a small step and w0 is the coordinate we are measuring distance with respect
to [4].
Because the direction is computed through the metric G(w), taking a step with Eu-
clidean length 1 can correspond to steps of diﬀerent lengths on the manifold, depending
==PAGE:45==
3.2 Natural Gradient
37
.
Figure 3.2: A schematic of how “curvature” can aﬀect distance. Steps (black arrows)
in the parameter space (the plane) can be distorted in length (the red
arrows) by the underlying geometry..
on what direction you pick. So if we compute and follow a regular gradient on w we will
not take into regard the vastly diﬀerent length scales as we will be implicitly assuming
we are working in a standard Euclidean space. Intuitively, we want to scale down direc-
tions with long length scales and vice versa, leading us to the following deﬁnition of a
natural gradient:
Deﬁnition 3.2.1 (Natural Gradient). The natural gradient ˜∇L of a loss function L
deﬁned on a Riemannian manifold (M, g) with metric tensor G(w) for w ∈M is deﬁned
as
˜∇L(w) = G(w)−1∇L(w)
(3.29)
The above deﬁnition is a bit heuristic, but it turns out to make sense as we will now
prove.
Theorem 3.2.2. For a loss function L deﬁned on a Riemannian manifold x ∈M with
metric G(x), the direction of steepest ascent is given by the natural gradient ˜∇L.
Proof. This proof will be similar to our previous steepest descent proof. We deﬁne a
direction δw = αa, but impose the new constraint Dw(a)2 = 1. For a second order
Taylor expansion, the rate of increase is still given by ∇L(w)⊤a, meaning that we can
construct the following auxiliary objective by the method of Lagrange multipliers:
∇L(w)a + λ(1 −aG(w)a) = 0
(3.30)
Taking the gradient, we get ∇L(w) = 2λG(w)a, which leads us to deﬁning
a = 1
2λG(w)−1∇L(w)
(3.31)
Although λ can be found by substitution it is a scalar and will not change the direction,
leading us to the conclusion that the natural gradient is the direction of steepest descent.
==PAGE:46==

Figure 3.3: Regular vs natural gradients.
The contours denote the KL divergence
between two beta distributions which appears to be poorly scaled. The
gradients point towards the axis of symmetry, while the natural gradients
point towards the center. The dashed paths denote “gradient ﬂows” with
the same color coding.
We are primarily interested in applying natural gradients in the context of probability
distributions. The statistical manifold S(Ω) for a domain Ωis deﬁned as the set of
measures µ on the domain. A parametrized family of probability distributions will form
a subset F ⊂S(Ω) in this space, which for well-behaved families will form a smooth
manifold.
It has been shown [38, 39] that the Fisher information matrix I is the only metric
which is invariant to re-parametrization for statistical manifolds. I.e., if I change my
coordinates by a linear transformation, ideally that should leave the distances between
two close points unchanged. This invariance property can in some sense be argued to
make it the most suitable metric for distribution manifolds, unless there is ample reason
to choose something diﬀerent. The same argument is used to defend Jeﬀrey’s prior as
the best choice of non-informative prior [6].
As an extra bit of encouragement, this is extremely convenient for variational infer-
ence when working with exponential family models. If we consider the expression we
found for the gradient in equation (3.26), then we just need to recall that ∇2A(ϕ) is
equal to the Fisher information for any exponential family, to see that if we premultiply
by the inverse Fisher, the two matrices cancel out leaving the simple expression
˜∇L(ϕ) = Eq[η] −ϕ.
(3.32)
==PAGE:47==
3.3 Nonparametric Variational Inference
39
3.2.1
Circumventing the Fisher Matrix
There is another trick we can employ if we are working with an exponential family;
namely its convex conjugate parametrization through the mean parameter µ.
To start a bit more generally, let us consider homeomorphic coordinate transfor-
mations of the form θ = ϕ(η). Using coordinate transformations, we can parametrize
an objective function in any form we like. To change from natural parameters η to a
diﬀerent parameter set θ we simply have to multiply by the Jacobian:
∇ηF(ϕ(η)) = Jϕ(η)⊤[∇θF](ϕ(η)).
(3.33)
For a coordinate transform function ϕ : RK →RN, the Jacobian Jϕ is an N × K matrix
of derivatives,
[Jϕ(η)]ij = ∂ϕi
∂ηj
.
(3.34)
Now consider a particular transform, namely:
µ = ϕ(η) = ∇A(η) ⇒Jϕ(η) = ∇2A(η) = I(η).
(3.35)
We recognize this gradient map to be the one that maps natural parameters to expec-
tation parameters µ. As illustrated, a transformation of this type for any exponential
family distribution leads to the Jacobian being equal to the Fisher matrix.
As the Fisher matrix is symmetric (being a Hessian matrix), we can calculate the
natural gradient again,
ˆ∇F(ϕ(η)) = I−1(η)∇F(ϕ(η)) = I−1(η)Jϕ(η)⊤[∇πF](ϕ(η)) = [∇πF](ϕ(η)),
(3.36)
and observe that the two Fisher matrices cancel out! So to calculate the natural gradient,
we just need to calculate the gradient for the expectation parameters.
We can perform the same calculation going the other way by using the two relations
I(π) = J⊤
ϕ−1Iη(ϕ−1(π))Jϕ−1 = Jϕ−1,
Jϕ−1 = J−1
ϕ
(3.37)
Using this, we have (for F parametrized in η)
ˆ∇F(ϕ−1(π)) = J−1
ϕ−1J⊤
ϕ−1[∇ηF](ϕ−1(π)) = [∇ηF](ϕ−1(π))
(3.38)
This above bit means that we can compute the natural gradient implicitly by cal-
culating the gradient in the mean parametrization. For some distributions, e.g. the
multinomial, this can be a much simpler aﬀair if we cannot compute the expectation in
equation 3.32.
3.3
Nonparametric Variational Inference
While nonparametric distributions are undeniably powerful distribution objects, they
also have the severe limitation that they are often formulated in terms of inﬁnite sums
or parameter sets.
==PAGE:48==

3 Variational Inference

inﬁnite set of parameters with a variational approximate distribution.
Let us in particular consider the stick-breaking construction of the Dirichlet process
from equation (2.67). It spans an inﬁnite number of atomic delta distributions meaning
that we will need an inﬁnite number of variational distributions to approximate it.

3.3.1
Inference Procedures
Blei and Jordan were probably the ﬁrst to propose variational inference for nonpara-
metrics in [7]. Working with the stick-breaking representation, they make the argument
that we do not need to settle for a truncation level during the model speciﬁcation i.e.
by directly setting the prior to be a truncated process as we considered in the previous
section.
Instead, we model each stick-breaking variable βk with a Beta distribution q(vk) =
Beta(a, b), except for the T’th stick vT, where T is the desired truncation level. For vT
we will explicitly model it as a delta distribution q(vT) = δ1(vT) so that q(vT = 1) = 1.
Due to the nature of the stick-breaking process this automatically makes every stick
later in the process default to 0.
An issue with this approach is that it does not scale naturally with the truncation
level. If the truncation level is moved, the entire sequence of stick-breaking weights
have to be re-calculated to re-distribute the probability mass correctly. We are also not
guaranteed that the variational bound improves as we increase the truncation level T;
rather, T has become an explicit parameter of the variational bound.
This leads us to the concept of nestedness [34]. A truncation approach can be said
to be nested if changing the truncation level only changes the stick-breaking weights
directly added or removed by the truncation. In the nested approach we again model
every stick-breaking parameter under T by a beta distribution, but instead of cutting
==PAGE:50==
42
3 Variational Inference
the distribution of at T we just set every q(vk) for k ≥T to be the prior distribution [34].
If the model is well-behaved, this will lead to tractable (ﬁnite) forms for the ELBO. This
formulation allows us to adapt the truncation level as part of the optimization process.
We will stick to the hard truncation approach from [7], although the nested approach
is promising.
3.4
Stochastic Variational Inference
In 2013, Hoﬀman et al. published an article on stochastic variational inference (SVI) [20]
which has since led to a large number of articles studying scalable variational inference,
many using the framework they proposed [17, 45, 19, 47].
SVI is motivated by the notion that variables generally come in two types: global
parameters τ which deﬁne the model, and local contexts (zi, xi) which determine local
behavior and, more importantly, scale with the problem. The SVI literature[20] makes
the argument that it is wasteful to update all the local contexts using a poorly tuned
model, and then update the model parameters to ﬁt local contexts tuned to a wrong
setting. Instead, SVI argues the quantities should be updated concurrently and in small
steps.
SVI makes the underlying assumption that the joint distribution factorizes as
p(τ, z, x) = p(τ)
N
∏
i=1
p(zi|τ)p(xi|zi)
(3.48)
where we reiterate the distinction between the global variables τ and the local variables
(zi, xi), where xi is observed, and z is a latent variable.
In a variational setting, we want to pick the optimal setting for the global approxima-
tion q(τ; λ) before we even consider the more expensive optimization of local variational
approximations q(zi; ϕi). This is equivalent to optimizing the composite objective:
ˆλ = arg max
λ
L(λ, ϕ) ◦g(λ),
g(λ) = (λ, ϕmax(λ)),
ϕmax(λ) = arg max
ϕ
L(λ, ϕ)
where g(λ) is a vector-valued variable substitution, ◦is the function composition opera-
tor, and ϕmax(λ) is an implicitly deﬁned function which always picks the best possible
setting of ϕ for any λ.
∇ˆL = ∇[L ◦g] = [JL ◦g]Jg = ([∇λL] ◦g, [∇ϕL] ◦g)⊤(I, J(ϕmax)
g
) =
(3.49)
[∇λL] ◦g + ([∇λL] ◦g)J(ϕmax)
g
= [∇λL] ◦g
(3.50)
where the last equation follows from the fact that [∇λL] ◦g = 0 since g puts the
objective in an extrema with respect to λ.
Using the above relation, we can maximize the global variables while disregarding the
local contexts by following the unmodiﬁed λ-gradient ∇λL, coupled with the variable

Figure 3.4: SVI assumes that the model has a particular form, namely that it can
