substitution g(λ). Of course, the gradient likely depends on the latent variables ϕ, which
we have implicitly maximized, so the decomposition is not complete.
The trick now is to exploit the conditional independence property, i.e.
zi, xi ⊥⊥
zj, xj|τ, ∀i, j. Due to this property the local contexts can be updated in isolation, as
changing the variables governing one local context will not aﬀect the extrema of another
context given their mutual conditional independence.
Hoﬀman et al. apply these principles to the general mean-ﬁeld variational objective
for a conjugate exponential family model with conditional independence properties as
in equation (3.48). For the global variables with approximation q(τ; λ) we can write the
λ-ELBO as
L(λ) = E[ln p(τ) −ln q(τ; λ)] +
N
∑
i=1
max
ϕi E[ln p(zi|λ) −ln q(zi; ϕi)]
(3.51)
This is arguably not much simpler as we have replaced a standard objective function by
a sum over implicit maximizations.
This is exploited in SVI by sampling a local context (zs, xs) from some sampling
distribution s ∼p(s). We can then show that
L(λ) = E[ln p(τ) −ln q(τ; λ)] +
1
p(s) max
ϕs E[ln p(zs|λ) −ln q(zs; ϕs)]
(3.52)
is an unbiased estimator of the true objective. So even though the correct gradient
requires an implicit maximization of all the local contexts, it suﬃces to maximize just a
single context at the time if we settle for a noisy gradient. In a sense we are working with
an implicit maximization over all local contexts without actually having to compute it.
==PAGE:52==
44
3 Variational Inference
3.4.1
Black-Box Variational Inference
Although SVI can be very eﬀective, it makes a lot of assumptions about the structure
of the model.
This led to the development of black-box inference [45] which makes
it possible to conduct stochastic variational inference (in the general sense) on any
probabilistic model.
Black-box variational inference takes its oﬀset in the expectation-based formula for
the gradient of the ELBO we found earlier in proposition 3.1.3.
∇L(ϕ) = Eq[V(θ, ϕ)ℓ(θ, ϕ)]
Ranganath et al. argue that a simple way to construct an unbiased estimate of the
gradient is to take S samples θi ∼q(ϕ) and form a mean estimator
ˆ∇L(ϕ) =
S
∑
i=1
V(θi, ϕ)ℓ(θi, ϕ)
This is in an of itself a perfectly valid way to perform stochastic inference and builds on
the same assumptions as variational inference: q(θ; ϕ) should be a tractable distribution
and the score function V(θ, ϕ) = ∇ln q(θ; ϕ) should likewise be computable, which is
made a lot more tractable by making the mean-ﬁeld assumption.
The problem with the algorithm as presented is that it has incredibly high variance.
In [45] they propose two variance-reducing extensions, namely Rao-Blackwellization and
control variates.
Rao-Blackwellization is related to the Rao-Blackwell theorem which states that the
variance of an estimator is reduced if we take the expectation of the estimator conditioned
on a suﬃcient statistic [12].
In fact, even using an insuﬃcient statistic will lead to
reduced variance although it will also often lead to an improper estimator [12]. Building
on this, we can restate expectation of the gradient as an iterated expectation with respect
to the diﬀerent variational parameters ϕi, and then calculate the expectations directly
insofar as it is possible. In particular, we can disregard all variables not in the Markov
blanket. This is the same process we went through to ﬁnd the ϕi-ELBO leading to the
gradient expression
∇L(ϕi) = E[V(θi, ϕi) [ln p(θi|...) −ln q(θi; ϕi)]]
(3.53)
Since the expectation is over fewer variables, as we have removed everything but the
suﬃcient statistics (only θi and the variables in the Markov blanket remain) the variance
is lower by the Rao-Blackwell theorem.
The second idea was to use direct variance countrol, by augmenting the estimators
with control variates. The idea of a control variate is a bit unintuitive: it turns out you
can reduce the variance of an estimator by adding another stochastic term to it. How
this works is that if you have an unbiased estimator g, then you can choose an arbitrary
estimator h with E[h] = c and combine them to make the control-variate estimator
˜g = g −α(h −c)
(3.54)
==PAGE:53==
3.4 Stochastic Variational Inference
45
where α ≥0 is a free parameter. ˜g is an unbiased estimator of the same quantity as g as
the quantity h −c has expectation 0. What is interesting, though, is the variance which
can be computed as
Var(˜g) = Var(g) + α2 Var(h) −2α Cov(g, h)
(3.55)
If we minimize this with respect to the free parameter, we ﬁnd that if we set
α = Cov(g, h)
Var(h)
then the variance reduces to
Var(g) −Cov(g, h)2
Var(g)
This means that we can reduce the variance by ﬁnding an estimator h which is highly
covariant with g and has as low variance as possible.
In [45] they propose using the score function V as it always has expectation 0 and
since it is bound to correlate well with the gradient. Another proposal for a control-
variate is the covariance matrix which can be estimated with lower variance and has
been shown to yield good results in a similar setup [48].
==PAGE:54==
CHAPTER 4
Relational Models
In machine learning, it can be interesting to work with relationships between objects
instead of the objects themselves. The fundamental mathematical object for describing
relationships is called a graph or, more colloquially, a network.
A graph is an abstract mathematical structure for describing relationships between
things. At its most basic, a graph consists of two sets: a set of vertices V which represent
the abstract entities of concern, and a set of edges E which represent the relationships
between the vertices. A bit more formally, each e ∈E is a 2-tuple (i, j) where i, j ∈V .
If a tuple (i, j) is in the set E it means there is an edge between nodes i and j.
We will focus on simple graphs in this thesis. For a graph to be simple it has to
fulﬁll three conditions: the edges must all be undirected, there cannot be more than one
edge between any two elements, and a node cannot connect to itself. That a graph is
undirected means that for any edge (i, j) the connection is symmetric: there is no start
nor end vertex.
Although the set notation for graphs has its merits, it is a bit cumbersome for
machine learning.
A common alternative representation is known as the adjacency
matrix which is an ordinary matrix A|V |×|V | where Aij = |{e | e ∈E, e = (i, j)}|. Note
that for simple graphs the set cardinality reduces to a simple indicator function so that
Aij = 1[(i, j) ∈E] and all elements will be either 0 or 1. Furthermore, the constraint
against self-loops means that the diagonal will be 0 and the undirectedness requirement
means that the matrix will be symmetrical. For a simple graph with N = |V |, there
are |E| ≤N(N−1)
2
possible edges, so for arbitrary graphs the number of edges |E| scales
quadratically in the number of nodes N. We say that an edge is sparse if it instead
grows linearly in N.
Graph theory is sometimes conﬂated with network science, but they have diﬀer-
ent goals in mind. While graph theory is concerned with the theoretical properties of
abstract graphs, network science is interested in analyzing the structure of real-world
networks. Confusingly, network science often refers to graphs as networks, vertices as
nodes, and edges as links, but the mathematical object is fundamentally the same. We
will use the terms interchangeably.
4.1
Bayesian Nonparametric Relational Models
As with any other Bayesian model, a statistical network model places a probability
distribution over the quantity of interest, which is in this case a graph. If we constrain
ourselves to simple graphs of N vertices then such a distribution is equivalent to a
probability distribution over edge variables p(e). This is almost universally modeled as
==PAGE:55==
4.1 Bayesian Nonparametric Relational Models
47
a product of conditionally independent Bernoulli distributions:
p(e|ρ) =
∏
E
Ber(eij|ρij)
(4.1)
where ρij is the probability of the edge. The modeling task then boils down to ﬁnding
a suitable latent distribution p(ρ). Often we only observe a single graph G so modeling
each ρij directly is an ill-speciﬁed model as we are left with as many parameters as
observations. Instead, we often suppose that there is some latent structure.
Latent structure can exist on multiple levels in a relational models: both vertices and
edges can possess latent characteristics. We will concern ourselves mostly with models
where the nodes have latent structure. First, though, we will return to the concept of
exchangeability, and see what it implies for graphs.
4.1.1
Exchangeable Graphs
The concept of exchangeability seems especially apt for graphs: if the nodes are reordered
the graph remains the same and so any probability distribution over graphs should
likewise remain unchanged. The ordering of the nodes and the rows and columns of the
corresponding adjacency matrix is completely arbitrary - only the relationships between
the vertices matter. Since the graph can be represented by an adjacency matrix, the
Aldous-Hoover theorem can be applied directly to ﬁnd an exchangeable representation.
For simple graphs, though, we can make some slight simpliﬁcations [43]:
Theorem 4.1.1. Let G be a random simple graph with vertex set N and let X be its
adjacency matrix. Then G is an exchangeable graph if and only if there is a random
function W from [0, 1]2 to [0, 1] such that
(Xij)
d= 1
[
U{i,j} < W(Ui, Uj)
]
(4.2)
where Ui and U{i,j} are i.i.d. uniformly distributed variables on [0, 1].
This representation makes everything very explicit: Ui and Uj pick out a connec-
tion probability p = W(Ui, Uj) from the random function W and U{i,j} then randomly
samples an edge with probability p. The representation could be equivalently stated as
Xij
d= Ber(W(Ui, Uj)) for the Bernoulli distribution Ber(p), which brings us back to the
naive Bernoulli representation we presented in the beginning.
An instantiation of W is a rather interesting object in an of itself referred to in the
literature as a graphon [43]. For a graph GN with |V | = N, we can deﬁne the empirical
graphon as wGN by splitting the [0, 1]2 unit square into a grid of N × N blocks and
ﬁlling block (i, j) in the grid with the value of the adjacency matrix Aij. Essentially we
just scale the adjacency matrix to the continuous unit square. If GN deﬁnes a family
of graphs with the same structure, the underlying graphon is in a particular sense the
limit object of wGN as N →∞and encapsulates the model structure of the graph.
A peculiar thing we have to bear in mind is that the idea of joint exchangeability
is at odds with the idea of sparsity. We call a graph sparse if |E| scales as O(N), i.e.
==PAGE:56==
48
4 Relational Models
the number of edges grows at most proportionally to the number of vertices. Yet the
expected proportion of edges given a graphon w is ρ =
1
2
∫
[0,1]2 w(x, y) dx dy.
For a
graph with N nodes the expected number of edges ρN(N−1)
2
scales as O(N 2) meaning
that it will not be sparse in the asymptotic sense. To get an intuition for why this is
problematic, this implies that if you only had 1 friend in a social network of a 100 people,
you would have ≈10.000 friends if the network got scaled up to a million users. While
this apparent misspeciﬁcation is worrisome, the truth is that for real-world networks it
is probably naive to expect the same graphon to account for both the small and the
large network anyway. The question is more if we can accurately model the network at
any given point in time.
4.1.2
Block Models
We will now consider a particularly pervasive class of network models, namely block
models. We will start by considering them in a general exchangeable graph framework
[43] before considering a particular kind of block model, namely the stochastic block
model (SBM). The SBM will serve as a stepping stone for the inﬁnite relational model
which is central to this thesis.
In the general setting, the class of models the block model belongs to is referred to
as a cluster-based model and to get to its deﬁnition, we will need two auxiliary concepts:
the notion of a simple cluster-based model and the notion of a P-randomization [43].
A model is simple cluster-based, when the random function F in the Aldous-Hoover
representation takes a particularly simple form, namely that it is “block-wise” constant.
Deﬁnition 4.1.2 (simple cluster-based model). A Bayesian model of a graph G is simple
cluster-based if the following is true for the Aldous-Hoover random function F:
• For partitions B1, B2, ... and C1, C2, ... of the unit interval [0, 1], the random func-
tion F is constant on the interval Ai,j = Bi × Cj.
• If fij is the value of F on the interval Aij then the array (fij) is itself exchangeable
and independent from the partitions.
It should be clear that this model is a bit too simple to deﬁne a graph, as edges do
not appear in perfect blocks. This leads us to introduce the notion of a P-randomization
[43]. A P-randomization in essence constructs a set of random variables as draws from
a distribution P with parameters indexed by another set of random variables.
Deﬁnition 4.1.3 (P-randomization). Let Pθ be a distribution parametrized by elements
in a space θ ∈Θ. Let θ = {θi ∈Θ : i ∈I} be a sequence of random variables indexed by
i. A P-randomization is then deﬁned as a set of random variables X = {Xi ∈X : i ∈I}
over the same index set, where Xi | θi ∼Pθi.
This immediately leads to the deﬁnition of a cluster-based model.
==PAGE:57==
4.2 Inﬁnite Relational Model
49
Deﬁnition 4.1.4 (cluster-based model). A Bayesian graph model for an exchangeable
graph is cluster-based when the edge variables eij are a P-randomization of a simple
cluster-based exchangeable array (θij).
This brings us back to the the statistical framework we presented in the beginning of
this chapter. If P is the Bernoulli distribution then the constraint imposed by a cluster-
based model is that the connection probabilities ρij should be simple cluster-based and
exchangeable.
The Stochastic Block Model, which has served to inspire a lot of the other models
we will later consider, ﬁts neatly into this framework as we mentioned earlier.
The Stochastic Block Model assumes that the vertex set of a graph can be partitioned
into K non-overlapping subsets Bk (the blocks), such that ∪K
k Bk = V . The presence of
an edge is then determined solely by the group memberships of its vertices, such that
if the vertices are members of blocks k and ℓthe probability of them connecting is ρkℓ.
Writing this as a hierarchical Bayesian model we get
zi ∼Mult(π)
ρkℓ∼Beta(η)
eij|zi, zj, ρ ∼Ber(ρzi,zj)
The way this ﬁts into the exchangeability framework is by noting that the two ﬁrst
steps together describe an exchangeable simple cluster-based model: if we split the
[0, 1] × [0, 1] unit square into blocks Akℓof size πk × πℓthen the random draws of ρkℓ
constitute the value of the corresponding block yielding a graphon of simple cluster-based
type.
Cluster-based models and block models are useful as performing inference induces a
partition of the nodes, which makes it a very suitable model for community detection.
It remains a popular model and an active ﬁeld of research, but is limited by the strong
model impact of the cluster count parameter K which necessitates rigorous model se-
lection. This hindrance is also faced by the similarly popular K-means for vector space
clustering.
We will not go into further detail with the Stochastic Block Model.
4.2
Inﬁnite Relational Model
The Inﬁnite Relational Model was ﬁrst described by Kemp et al.[30], and is a non-
parametric extension of the Stochastic Block Model. It employs the Dirichlet process to
induce a partition over vertices in much the same way that the Dirichlet process mixture
induces a partition over data points [30, 22, 43].
The model consists of four sets of parameters:
Weight vk describes the relative prior probability for a vertex to belong to cluster k.
Connectivity ρkℓdescribes the probability of an edge between vertices in clusters k
and ℓ.

Equation (4.16) opens up for an alternative interpretation of the model as a mixture
of simple graph models [57]. The clustering on the nodes induces a clustering on the
edges, and all edges in the same cluster have the same probability of occurring.
A
Bayesian relational model where all the edges are independent and equally likely is
known as an Erdos-Renyi graph model.
4.2.2

The Collapsed Inﬁnite Relational Model (CIRM) can be directly derived from the IRM
by marginalizing out the model parameters τ = (v, ρ).

considered in section 4.2.1, then this integral simpliﬁes considerably:

These expressions are further simpliﬁed by the condensed representation we derived in

makes sense as multiplying by a conjugate prior should lead to a distribution of the
same functional form. Integrating the kernel of a distribution with a known normalizer
is almost trivial as you just substitute the normalizer. In the case of a beta distribution,
the result is simply the Beta function:
∫

(4.21)

While superﬁcially attractive in that it is a very succinct representation, a lot of new
dependencies have been introduced and the Beta functions make the interactions a bit
more obscure. Generally, the Beta function is decreasing and it takes on a higher value
if one of its arguments dominates.
4.3
Mixed-Membership Block Model
The mixed-membership block model [2] extends the stochastic block model so that each
node can belong to several of K clusters. The hierarchical model description can be
stated as
πi ∼Dir(α), ∀i ∈V
(4.22)
ρkℓ∼Beta(ν), ∀k, ℓ∈K × K
(4.23)
zi→j | πi ∼Mult(πi), ∀i, j ∈V × V
(4.24)
eij | zi→j, zj→i, ρ ∼Ber(ρzi→j,zj→i), (i, j) ∈E
(4.25)
The generative story to go with it goes as follows.
1. The clusters are connected with connectivity strengths ρkℓ
2. From a Dirichlet prior, each node i draws a distribution over the diﬀerent clusters
πi.
==PAGE:62==

Figure 4.2: A small part of a graphical model for the a-MMSB. This snapshot focuses
on the variables relevant to a single edge eij. .
3. For each edge, the incident nodes i and j decide on a “message” by drawing
zi→j ∼πi and zj→i ∼πj.
4. For that particular edge, the nodes are considered to be in the cluster denoted by
the messages drawn in the previous step, and will connect with a probability given
by ρzi→j,zj→i.
While this model is ﬂexible, it is very parameter-intensive and we will instead consider an
interesting simpliﬁcation known as the assortative mixed-membership stochastic block
model (a-MMSB) [17].
Assortativity is a possible assumption in network modeling [26] which presumes that
nodes connect if they have similar latent properties. Applying this assumption, we set
ρkℓ= ϵ if k ̸= ℓand deﬁne ρk ≡ρkk to form the a-MMSB. Here, ϵ is a very small positive
probability, encouraging assortative behaviour - the only way to make an edge probable
is by making it likely that the connected nodes inhabit the same cluster k.
πi ∼Dir(α), ∀i ∈V
(4.26)
ρk ∼Beta(ν), ∀k ∈K
(4.27)
zi→j | πi ∼Mult(πi), ∀i, j ∈V × V
(4.28)

ϵ

(4.29)
eij | zi→j, zj→i, ρ ∼Ber(wij), (i, j) ∈E
(4.30)
We will assume that zi→j is coded in a 1-of-K manner as a K-length vector where
zi→j,k = 1 if the sampled message was k and 0 otherwise.

Example: Exact Inference in the CIRM
Let us consider the problem of inference for the collapsed model:
p(z|e) =
p(e|z)p(z)
∑
z p(e|z)p(z)
(4.31)
While less intimidating than an integral, the sum in the denominator is still over all
possible conﬁgurations. If we truncate the model at T and have N nodes, there are still
T N possible conﬁgurations, which is an intractable amount.
But let us consider a small graph with N = 3 and a single edge between nodes 1 and
2, which we proceed to model with a CIRM with only T = 3 clusters. Then we only
have 33 = 27 conﬁgurations.
Some conﬁgurations will share counting statistics. Since the labels induce a partition
into clusters, we can consider the three possible cluster conﬁgurations: either all the
nodes are in the same cluster (1 partition), all the nodes are in diﬀerent clusters (1
partition), or two nodes are grouped together and the last by itself (3 diﬀerent partitions,
depending on which node is left out). If they are all grouped together we can choose
one of the 3 labels for the group, leading to three conﬁgurations. If they are grouped
separately we can choose a label freely for each group as long as they are all diﬀerent
leading to 6 conﬁgurations. Finally, for each of the 3 two-to-one splits we can again pick
the labels freely for each of the sub-partitions leading to 6 conﬁgurations per partition, for
a grand total of 18. Adding these numbers together we count 27 possible conﬁgurations.
There are only 6 diﬀerent pairings of clusters k and ℓ, so there are only 6 possible
values for the nkℓstatistics. For the membership counts, the m statistic can take on
3 settings if all nodes are in the same cluster, 1 setting if they are all in separate
clusters (i.e. (1, 1, 1)), and only 3 settings if they are in a two-to-one conﬁguration as
the conﬁgurations are indistinguishable. This leaves the membership statistic m to take
on 10 diﬀerent values.
With some idea of what the diﬀerent clusterings entail, we can compute each term
of the sum in the denominator of equation (4.31). To simplify the calculations further,
we will set the hyperparameters α and β to 1, which yields the following simpliﬁed form
of the Beta function:
B(1 + x, 1 + y) =
x! y!
(x + y + 1)!
(4.32)

the non-edges column we list two such tuples, one for each of the non-edges.

more probable when points are assigned to low-indexed clusters and the more homoge-

many of the terms have the same probability due to symmetries and similarities in the

We have then essentially solved the Bayesian inference problem by recovering the

require an intractable amount of computation, leaving us to consider other means of
inference.
==PAGE:66==
CHAPTER 5
Variational Inference for
Relational Models
We now return to the variational problem, but in the speciﬁc context of relational models.
As before, the problem is to maximize an evidence lower bound (ELBO) L(q) ≤ln p(e)
which is a functional of the variational approximation q. As derived earlier, this in turn
minimizes the KL divergence between the approximation q and the true posterior.
The ﬁrst step in designing an algorithm for variational inference is to deﬁne the
lower bound. When considering optimization, we often only need one variable at the
time, so we will focus on the conditional ELBO representations presented in equation
(3.19). This necessitates knowing the conjugate distribution, so we will make that our
ﬁrst priority.
5.1

5.1.1
Complete Conditional
Finding the complete conditional of each parameter turns out to be key, much as it is
for Gibbs sampling. If we have the complete conditional then we know the appropriate
functional form of the complete conditional and we can deduce the update step simply
by taking the expectation of the conditional distribution’s parameter. So in this section
we will pursue the complete conditionals.
Stick-breaking Weights
For the stick-breaking weights, we can exploit the condensed structure in equation (5.6).
p(vk|...) = p(v, z)
p(v\k, z) = Beta(vk; 1 + mk, α + m∞
k+1)
(5.10)
The integration is simple to carry out because of the conjugacy and the independent
factor structure, and the division makes all the other factors cancel out.
As the complete conditional is a Beta distribution, we should use a Beta distribution
for our variational approximation,
q(vk|λ0,k, λ1,k) = Beta(vk|λ0,k, λ1,k)
(5.11)
which can be substituted into the λk-ELBO L(λ0,k, λ1,k), to yield

exploit that we are working with an exponential family again. The distribution q(ρkℓ) is
a beta distribution which means it has suﬃcient statistics ln(ρkℓ) and ln(1 −ρkℓ). These
are related to the log-normalizer by the relation ∇A(ξkℓ) = E[T(ρkℓ)], so to compute the
expectations we can equivalently compute the gradient
∇ln B(ξ0,kℓ, ξ0,kℓ) = ∇ln Γ(ξ0,kℓ) + ∇ln Γ(ξ1,kℓ) + ∇ln Γ(ξ0,kℓ+ ξ1,kℓ).
The function ψ(x) = ∇ln Γ(x) is called the digamma function and it is conveniently
available in most numerical computing packages.
5.2
Inference for the Mixed-membership Model
For the a-MMSB inference proceeds in much the same manner as for the IRM, but we
will detail the inference process to make the diﬀerences more apparent later.
5.2.1
Complete Conditional
As for the IRM, we again seek out the complete conditionals of the various parameters.
Since the a-MMSB is also a conjugate exponential family the parameter update is simply
equal to the expectation of the parameters.
Membership Distribution
The distribution over community memberships πi for all nodes i only occurs in two ways
in the joint distribution: in the prior, and in all outgoing messages zi→j. So the following
result holds up to proportionality
p(πi|...) ∝Dir(πi; α)
∏
j̸=i
Mult(zi→j; πi)
(5.31)
Since the Dirichlet and the multinomial form a conjugate pair, we can infer that the
complete conditional is a Dirichlet distribution parametrized as
p(πi|...) = Dir(πi; α +
∑
j̸=i
zi→j)
(5.32)
where we recall that zi→j is coded as a vector.
Since the complete conditional takes the form of a Dirichlet, we can approximate it
with a variational Dirichlet distribution q(π; λ) = Dir(π; λ), yielding the update equa-
tions:
λk ←αk +
∑
j̸=i
E[zi→j]
(5.33)

5.3
Natural Gradient Descent
We gave the general deﬁnition of the natural gradient for an exponential family in
equation (3.32) as
∇ηL = E[η] −η(t−1)
(5.51)
It was noted by Sato [49] that this update formula is exactly equivalent to the VB
updates of equation (3.27) when the step size is 1, as
η(t) = η(t−1) + ∇ηL = E
[
η(t)]
(5.52)
which connects the two update types. For quadratic models, a step size of 1 is usually
associated with a Newton step which can be seen as circumstantial evidence that the
natural gradient is a well-scaled update. Unfortunately, the VB steps are only valid
when performed coordinate-wise so taking a gradient step of 1 is not equivalent to a
cycle of VB updates due to the dependencies between the variables.
Since the formula of (3.32) is quite simple, we will only brieﬂy detail how natural
gradients are constructed for each of our models.

5.4
Stochastic Variational Inference
We now turn to stochastic variational inference, as presented in [20]. In particular, we
will consider its tried and true application to mixed-membership models [17].
First, we need to identify the local parameters. From ﬁgure 5.1(a) we can see that
the Markov blanket of a single message zi→j does not cover any other messages except
for the one from the opposite node zj→i. As such, these two messages together with the
edge make up a local context ℓij = (zi→j, zj→i, eij) as the local contexts are conditionally
independent given the global parameters g = (π, ρ).
==PAGE:75==
5.4 Stochastic Variational Inference

Figure 5.1: Comparison of the dependency properties of the (a) a-MMSB versus the (b)
IRM. The structure of a-MMSB makes it particularly amenable to SVI as
local context are conditionally independent. In the IRM each assignment
variable depends on every other assignment variable through the common
edge eij.
To design an SVI optimization algorithm, we need to consider how the problem
reduces if we sample a single local context ℓij from a sampling distribution i, j ∼p(i, j).
The distributions over the variables in the local context ﬁrst have to be maximized.
We note that this cannot be done in closed form since zi→j and zj→i are both in the local
context, so that we have to optimize them in unison. It is a small optimization problem,
though, and we have access to ﬁxed-point optimization in the form of the coordinate
ascent updates for the two variables.
With the local context maximized, an unbiased natural gradient estimator for the
global variables will point in the direction of the optimal setting. We can construct
unbiased natural gradient estimators from equations (5.57) and (5.58) as [17]. For con-
venience we deﬁne the expected message as mi→j,k = E[zij,k]
ˆ˜∇(λi,k) = αk +
1
p(i, j)mi→j,k −λk
ˆ˜∇L(ξ0,k) = ν0 +
1
p(i, j)mi→j,kmj→i,keij −ξ0,k,
ˆ˜∇(ξ1,k) = ν1 +
1
p(i, j)mi→j,kmj→i,keij −ξ1,k
For a local context ℓij we only need to update λi, λj as well as ξ. This means we
only have to update a small subset of the parameters in each iteration, making these
gradients extremely cheap to compute without violating their validity as estimators -
since they are unbiased they ﬁt neatly into the Robbins-Munro algorithm.
==PAGE:76==
68
5 Variational Inference for Relational Models
Turning to the IRM, we run into a problem as it lacks the basic structure necessary
for SVI. As the markov blanket of each assignment variable zi covers all variables in the
model (see ﬁgure 5.1(b)) there is no way to deﬁne a sensible local context. If we apply
the SVI methodology anyway, we’ll ﬁnd that to optimize the “local context” we have to
optimize all of the assignment variables which is an intractable task.
We could turn to black-box stochastic variational inference [45], but IRM is still a
conjugate exponential family model, despite its lack of the correct conditional indepen-
dence structure, so black-box inference might be making too few assumptions in this
particular case. Either way, black-box inference is just a low-hassle way to ﬁnd gradi-
ent estimators; we already have formulas for the natural gradients, and can look into
designing proper estimators for those formulas.
5.4.1
Unbiased Estimators
An estimator is a function which produces an estimate of some quantity from a sample.
An unbiased estimator is simply an estimator where the mean coincides with the quantity
of interest. E.g. if we have a quantity K, then an estimator δ is unbiased if E[δ] = K.
Looking at the natural gradient expressions in section 5.3.1, it is noticeable that all
the terms depend linearly on the counting statistics. In other words, if we can provide
unbiased estimates of the counting statistics, unbiased estimates of the natural gradients
will follow naturally. This makes it possible to apply Robbins-Monro optimization in a
sensible manner.
The counting statistics are functions of the N latent variables zi, which continue
to be T-dimensional assignment vectors. The latent variables are independent, so the
expectation operator distributes over both sums and products, so we can express the
expectations of the statistics in terms of expectations of the latent variables z. Each zi is
drawn from a multinomial distribution, so it is simple to compute its mean. As the mean
doesn’t coincide exactly with the parameters ϕ because we are using a minimal natural
parametrization, we will denote the expectations by E[zi] = µi for ease of reading.
As a simple ﬁrst case, let us consider the membership count E[mk] (cf. equation
(5.8)). We are free to introduce randomness in any way we see ﬁt in an eﬀort to reduce
the amount of computation. Since T ≪N in our use case, we will consider sampling
over N.
For an arbitrary sampling distribution p(s) over s = 1, ..., N, a ﬁrst stab at an
estimator could be the simple ˆmk = Csµs,k where Cs is a correction factor. Taking the
expectation, we get
E[ ˆmk(s)] =
N
∑
i=1
p(i)Ciµi,k
(5.60)
From this, we ﬁnd that if we set Cs = p(s)−1 the estimator becomes unbiased! This
makes sense intuitively if we consider uniform sampling: p(s) = 1
N . Then the estimator
is Nµs,k implying that since all samples are equally likely the best estimate given a single
sample is to assume all N terms are identical.

Note we left out the constant factor of 1/|E|2. Since we want low variance an ideal

Three strategies present themselves
split them highly covariant terms should not co-occur as they will contribute heavily
to the total variance.
sample them if two covariant terms have to co-occur, sample the set often. Since we
are weighting by the inverse of p(S) small probability terms have increased impact.
distribute them as we divide by the number of clusters ns(X), a ﬁnal strategy is
to distribute elements with high variance or covariance across many clusters to
distribute their inﬂuence.

ns(ei).
(5.76)
So with a uniform distribution on the sets the only variable left to tune is the design
of the sets themselves. Formula (5.75) indicates two things:
1. Too many sets will lead to increased variance.
2. Ideally, each pair should co-occur rarely, but each element should be in as many
sets as possible.
So when do two edges result in gradient estimates with high variance? Since the gra-
dients are determined by the counting statistics, we need to consider when the counting
statistics are similar. First oﬀ, if we consider two edges, then their estimators are going
to have high covariance if their distribution over clusters q(zi) is similar. But this can
be a hard thing to control for as it changes over time as the algorithm reassigns nodes
to new clusters. A second option is to consider the eﬀect of the binary edge variable e
(its parity). If it takes on diﬀerent parities for two edges the covariance between them is
going to be signiﬁcantly lower as the gradients will be orthogonal with respect to the con-
nectivity parameters. This same principle can be applied to the assignment parameters:
the more diﬀerent the edge proﬁle of the sampled nodes, the lower the covariance.

consistently with sample size though.
Node-stratiﬁed Sampling
Node-stratiﬁed sampling was proposed in [17] where it was shown to lead to good per-
formance. The idea is to make the updates more local and to even the inﬂuence between
the links and the non-links through stratiﬁcation. To make the process local, we make
a set for each node containing its incident edges.
Next, we impose stratiﬁcation by
subdividing each set into a set of links and a set of non-links. Finally, since there are
often vastly more non-links than links, we further subdivide each set of non-links into m
smaller sets. So the sampling process has three steps: pick a node, pick an edge parity,
and (if sampling non-links) pick a subset. Each sample is uniform, so the distribution
follows straightforwardly:
p(S) =
{
1

if non-link set

Reviewing this sampling scheme using the theory we have developed, it seems to go
against the design recommendations we detailed. It explicitly groups edges with the
same parity, meaning higher covariance, and by enforcing locality the sampled nodes are
likely to share similar assignment distributions as well.

Edges with diﬀerent parity will never co-occur, resulting in a weight of zero. Links
will co-occur exactly twice if they share a node. Non-links can potentially co-occur either
once or twice, depending on whether the non-links are assigned to the same blocks. As
such the variance has been set to 0 for a lot of terms, but the remaining terms see a
sharp increase to their covariance coeﬃcient in return through the outer factor of N/2,
and by our analysis we expect those terms to have particularly high a priori correlation.
Parity-Matched Sampling
A simple way to ensure that elements in the same set are less covariant is by collecting
edges with diﬀerent edge parity. If one edge is a link and the other is a non-link their
corresponding gradient estimates will be less correlated by virtue of the parity controlling
which subset of the connectivity and the assignment parameters should be updated.
Let us consider a partition of the set of all possible edges E into parity sets of links
E+ and non-links E−. The idea of parity-matching is to take an even amount of nodes
from each parity set: a sample S−of size k−from E−and a sample S+ of size k+ from
E+. Combining the two independent samples, we can form the sample set S = S−∪S+
of size k−+ k+.

sets has inherently higher variance than the other this could be an advantage, but it
does require more intimate knowledge of the covariance.
5.5
Collapsed Inference
Gibbs sampling of clustering methods is problematic as it acts at a low granularity - we
move a single data point at a time. If a proposed cluster is e.g. a merging of two actual
clusters, moving one point to a new cluster is likely to be a low-probability move, even
though splitting the cluster in two would overall be a good move. Part of the reasoning
for this is that the model parameters are updated in sequence and not in parallel, so
that poorly tuned clusters restrict moves to better clusters [54, 22].
This is likely to aﬀect VB as well, as the connection parameters of a cluster can
potentially obscure the underlying graph clustering. For instance, if we have two weakly
connected dense blocks which are by chance assigned to the same cluster, then the
internal connection probability will be concentrated around 0.5 and moving any member
of the cluster out to form a new cluster will make the original cluster less probable.
A number of approaches have been proposed to solve this, including blocked Gibbs
samplers[24], split-and-merge moves[27], and collapsed models[54]. We will explore the
latter in a VB context.
”Collapsed” is largely synonymous with marginalized, and is used to describe models
where nuisance variables have been marginalized out. This creates new dependencies
between the remaining variables, but removes dependencies on the nuisance variables.
==PAGE:85==
5.5 Collapsed Inference
77

mula and have yielded surprisingly good results. Even more surprising, CVB0 often
outperforms the higher-order CVB estimate [22].
==PAGE:88==

5 Variational Inference for Relational Models
In our opinion, the CVB is problematic because we are making low-order estimates
with regards to a ﬁxed point optimization formula, and we can thus no longer guarantee
that we are reliably optimizing the problem. Fortunately, there is another more mathe-
matically rigorous way of going about this, namely by deriving an alternate bound. This
bound turns out to be functionally equivalent to the CVB bound, without resorting to
approximations.
5.5.2
KL Correction
The Kullback-Leibler (KL) correction was pioneered in [32] for gaussian processes and
extended to general conjugate exponential family models by Hensman et al. [18].
The KL-corrected ELBO is an alternative lower bound on the log-evidence which is
inherently related to the ELBO and to the collapsed inﬁnite relational model.
We ﬁrst construct a bound on the conditional marginal log-likelihood, conditioned2
on the model parameters τ = (v, ρ):
ln p(e|τ) ≥L1 ≡Eq(z)[ln p(e, z|τ)] −Eq(z)[ln q(z)] .
(5.111)
Since this inequality is consistent under linear operations, we can apply the operation
ln Ep(τ)[exp(·)] to both sides to get the KL-corrected bound,

We should also make it clear that the KL-corrected bound is equivalent to the eﬀec-
tive objective function of a CVB0 approximation, so the two approaches are intimately
related [18].
5.5.3
KL-corrected Inference
Due to the loss of conjugacy, collapsed models are generally less amenable to mean-ﬁeld
coordinate ascent, as we saw in the previous section.
This leaves us to rely on more general alternatives such as ﬁrst-order gradient al-
gorithms. As luck would have it, it turns out that KL-corrected bounds are actually
well-suited for this kind of optimization as they have a high curvature [18], which is the
property that to some degree ensures that e.g. convex problems are easy to optimize.
As a ﬁrst step, let us consider the gradient (with respect to the parameters θ of the
variational approximation q(z; θ)) in the general setting of equation (5.112)
∇LKL = ∇ln
∫
p(τ) exp(L1) dτ =
∫p(τ) exp(L1)

(5.121)

in equation (5.117). Using the relationship between LMF and L1 detailed in equation
(5.114), we can compare with
∇LMF = Eq(τ)[∇L1]
(5.122)
revealing that the gradients are identical if q(τ) is optimized.
To conﬁrm the claim we made that the curvature is indeed higher for the latent
assignment parameters ϕ, we ﬁnd the curvature of the LMF bound by taking the gradient
of the gradient and moving the operator inside the expectation

Vq∗(τ, ϕ) = ∇ln q∗(τ|ϕ) = ∇ln p(τ) + ∇(L1 −LKL) = ∇L1 −Eq∗(τ|λ)[∇L1]

where the ﬁrst term is identical to the Hessian of the ELBO, while the two last terms
combine into the covariance matrix of the gradient ∇L1 which is a positive-deﬁnite
matrix. Thus the KL-corrected bound must have higher curvature, although it is still
non-convex as the ﬁrst term can dominate the latter.
5.5.4
KL Correction for the IRM
Since the IRM is a conjugate exponential family model, we can derive both LKL and
the auxiliary conditional bound L1 in closed form. The IRM conditioned on the model
parameters τ can be found by combining equations (4.10) and (4.16) and has the rather
succinct factorized form

parameters. This expression is noticeably identical to the natural gradients we devel-

substituted in, by way of the expectation over the optimal distribution q∗.
This realization makes the result somewhat less exciting as it is equivalent to itera-
tively running coordinate ascent on the model parameters and then gradient ascent on
the assignment parameters. Casting it as pure gradient ascent does allow us to pull on
more of the optimization literature though.
The connection unfortunately makes it diﬃcult to develop stochastic gradients for
the expression since there are nonlinear interactions through q∗.
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 6
Implementation
This short chapter will cover how the subjects we have covered so far can and have been
implemented. We will also consider the computational complexity of the implementa-
tions.
All of the code for this thesis was written in the programming language Python.
Python sees widespread use in the machine learning community, partly due to its ex-
tensive numerical computation module Numpy. By using Numpy, a programmer gets
to combine most of the beneﬁts of scripting languages like Matlab with the power of a
full-ﬂedged object-oriented programming language. Python also takes pride in having
intuitive and approachable code.
6.1
Structure - an Object-Oriented Approach
Like other Bayesian learning tasks, variational inference requires a rather comprehensive
framework of components. Arguably, this makes it well-suited for an object-oriented
approach which we will take here. In an object-oriented approach the general problem
is broken down into subunits (i.e. classes) and subtasks (i.e. functions and methods).
We break the variational algorithm up into the following co-dependent but compart-
mentalized parts.
ModelLearner This class manages the other classes. Everything related to iterations,
training, and evaluation goes here. It is also the primary user interface.
Model Represents the model we are trying to learn, e.g. an IRM. This class is respon-
sible for self-maintenance when issued updates to its parameters.
Scheduler This is another control class which allows us to update each parameter set
independently while also allowing shared behavior. The Scheduler is called in each
update iteration and then calls the individual updaters.
UpdateStep This class is an optimization algorithm for a parameter set. Subclasses
include CoordinateAscent and NaturalGradientAscent.
VariationalBound While the update algorithms determine how a parameter update
is applied, the VariationalBound provides all the information related to the
objective function. This includes coordinate ascent updates and gradient directions.
The VariationalBound is also responsible for calculating the bound itself.
==PAGE:96==
88
6 Implementation
6.2
Update Algorithm
For simple coordinate-ascent, the following constitutes an algorithmic description of the
approach we have detailed in previous chapters.
Algorithm 6 Coordinate-Ascent Updates for the Inﬁnite Relational Algorithm
1: repeat
2:
Update stickbreaking weight parameters λ
3:
Compute and store E[ln vk] and E[ln 1 −vk]
4:
Update connectivity parameters ξ
5:
Compute and store E[ln ρkℓ] and E[ln 1 −ρkℓ]
6:
for all indices i = 1, ..., N do
7:
Update node assignments ϕi
8:
Update counting statistics
9:
end for
10: until Convergence

6.3
Computational Complexity
When evaluating the eﬃciency of algorithms and implementations it is common to use
asymptotic notation. In particular, we will use asymptotic upper bounds O. If some
quantity f(n) has asymptotic upper bound O(g(n)) it implies that f(n) “grows slower”
than g(n). Mathematically, we have
Deﬁnition 6.3.1 (Asymptotic Upper Bound). For two functions f(n) and g(n), f(n)
is said to have asymptotic upper bound O(g(n)) when
∃K ∃N0 ∀N ≥N0 : f(N) ≤Kg(N)
(6.14)
for a coeﬃcient K and a threshold N0.
==PAGE:98==
90
6 Implementation
For polynomials in particular, this notation abstracts from everything but the order
of the dominating polynomial. E.g., if f(n) = ∑N
k=1 aknk then O(f(n)) is a trivial (tight)
upper bound, but since the highest-order term grows faster than the others O(aNnN)
is also an upper bound, and we can even pare it down to O(nN) due to the constant
present in the deﬁnition already.
In algorithmics, the function we are trying to upper bound is in most instances the
number of operations necessary to run the algorithm for a problem of size n. In our
case, n might be the number of edges or nodes in the graph. In that sense, the upper
bound denotes how well the algorithm scales as the size of the problem increases. How
the algorithm scales is often more informative than the exact number of computations
as the exact number would in all cases depend on the system it was implemented in. We
should note though, that the scaling coeﬃcient K has the potential to cause trouble -
even if an algorithm scales better, it might still be slower on most problems and only
beat its competitors for very large N0. Nevertheless, most algorithms do not exhibit
degeneracy of that order, and the computational complexity remains a good benchmark
for implementations and it has wide-reaching theoretical implications.
In our case, the two dominant inputs to our algorithms are the graph G = (V, E)
and the truncation level T. We note that since T is used to limit the number of node
partitions, and node partitions make more sense if they contain at least a few members,
it is likely the case that T < N.
6.3.1
Matrix Products
Since matrix products feature rather heavily and have a high computational impact we
are going to investigate their computational complexity before attacking the algorithm
proper.
A matrix product between two matrices AM×K and BK×N, with resulting matrix
CM×N, can be written as an element-wise operation
Cij =
K
∑
k=1
AikBkj
(6.15)
So if we have to do MN inner products of length K vectors, resulting in a computational
complexity of O(MKN), or simply O(N 3) for square matrices. There exists algorithms
which scale better than this such as Strassen’s algorithm, but they are rarely applicable
unless the problems are very big [35].
By applying the above logic, we can also deduce that an SVD-like triple product
AM×KBK×KCK×N has complexity O(K2(M + N)).
For sparse matrices, we need to compute the same amount of inner products, but
each inner product is potentially cheaper. If A in the previous has sparse rows with ki
non-zero elements and B is still dense, then we only need
M
∑
i=1
Nki = N
M
∑
i=1
ki = NMkavg
(6.16)
==PAGE:99==
6.3 Computational Complexity
91
operations where kavg is the average number of non-zero elements in any row.
In particular, if we are taking the product with respect to multiplication with a
graph’s adjacency matrix, the number of edges is exactly |E| = 1
2
∑N
i=1 ki since every
edge is accounted for twice (ki being the valence in graph terminology). So a product
between a M ×N matrix and a sparse N ×N matrix will have a complexity of O(N|E|).
6.3.2
Variational Inference Algorithm
Figuring out the complexity of our diﬀerent algorithms can be made simpler by realizing
that they are made of the same building blocks. So if we ﬁnd the complexity of the
building blocks, the complexity of the algorithms will follow.
We will consider four algorithms in this section: standard coordinate-ascent, Vari-
ational Bayes on the IRM, gradient-based optimization on the IRM, gradient-based
optimization on the CIRM, and stochastic gradient optimization on the IRM.
We make a small conjecture: the complexity is going to be dominated by the counting
operations.
6.3.3
Updates
Due to the rather tight connection between natural gradient and coordinate-ascent steps,
they have the same computational complexity. We have separated the updates from the
counting statistics as it will turn out the former are dominated by the latter. Never-
theless, we will investigate their complexity as it will come to matter when we consider
disregarding the computation of the counting statistics.
Model Parameters
The model parameters λ and ξ have rather simple update formulas, i.e.
λ0,k = 1 + E[nk],
λ1,k = α + E
[
n∞
k+1
]
ξ0,kℓ= β + E[nkℓ],
ξ1,kℓ= β + E[¯nkℓ].
If we have the expectations of the counting statistics memoized, the λ parameters can
be computed in O(T) additions and the ξ variables can be computed in O(T 2), where
T is the truncation level.
Assignment Parameter Update Complexity
Since the assignment variables are mutually dependent, we have to loop over them in
the coordinate descent paradigm. This leads to repeated application of the complicated
update formula from equation (5.30):
==PAGE:100==
92
6 Implementation
ϕi,k = E[ln ωi,k −ln ωi,T] = E
[
ln vk
vT
]
+
k−1
∑
ℓ=1
E[ln(1 −vℓ)] −
T−1
∑
ℓ=1
E[ln(1 −vℓ)]+
T
∑
ℓ=1
(
n(i)
ℓE
[
ln ρkℓ
ρTℓ
]
+ ¯n(i)
ℓE
[
ln 1 −ρkℓ
1 −ρTℓ
])
The two ﬁrst terms are only dependent on k and can thus be memoized and reused.
Their computation is on the order of O(T). We have to compute a sum with T terms
for each of the N × T parameters, so this gives a complexity of O(NT 2).
Counting Statistics
Computing the vector of membership counts mk is a sum over N elements T times, so
it has a complexity of O(NT). Computing the connectivity counts n(i)
ℓ
is equivalent to
a matrix product between an N × N matrix (the adjacency matrix G) and an N × T
matrix (the assignment expectations Θ) and thus has a (naive) complexity of O(N 2T).
While this is a true upper bound, it can be quite loose if the graph is sparse. To reﬂect
this we can rewrite the complexity in terms of the number of edges |E| in the graph,
yielding an equivalent asymptotic bound of O(|E|T). If a sparse graph grows as O(N)
then this is a fair bit tighter than our original proposal.
We can reuse this result to calculate the interaction counts nkℓ.
While they are
deﬁned as a double matrix product between a T × N, an N × N and an N × T matrix,
namely the product Θ⊤GΘ, the previous section dealt with the product GΘ, so we only
need consider the product Θ⊤C, which can be carried out in O(T 2N). This gives us a
per-iteration cost of O(|E|T + NT 2), but if this method is used as part of a coordinate-
ascent scheme, we have to compute these products every time we make a single update
to Φ. Multiplying by the number of vertices to update N, the upper bound resolves to
O(N|E|T + N 2T 2).
This is the naive way of doing it, and if we use low-rank updates instead, then the
connectivity count and edge count statistics can be computed by computing a single
outer product for each, using the formulas
C ←C + G:id⊤
M ←M + S ◦
(
dCi: + (dCi:)⊤)
.
(6.17)
The change vector d is of length T (the truncation level), and the slice through the
graph G is of length N, leaving us at O(NT) for the ﬁrst product. Since the slice is
through a graph, we can make the bound more explicit by replacing the N with the
valency ki. For the second product, both vector are length T so that is simply O(T 2).
As before, we still have to run these counting formulas each time we update one of the
N elements, so the resulting complexity ends up being O(|E|T + NT 2). So low-rank
updates reduce the complexity from cubic to quadratic in the worst-case.
The inverted graph counts ¯
M and ¯C are at the very worst as expensive to compute as
their non-accented counterparts since the same operations are required so the complexity
==PAGE:101==
6.3 Computational Complexity
93
is at worst unchanged. Fortunately, it turns out we can re-use a lot of the calcuations
from above, so the complexity ends up being lower.
6.3.4
Gradients
Performing the actual updates is no cheaper when using (natural) gradients, but the
complexity for the counting statistics is a bit diﬀerent. Since we are updating every
variational variable at the same time, we can no longer perform low-rank counting, but
on the other hand we only need to count once per iteration. Unfortunately, this amounts
to there being absolutely no computational advantage either way leaving us at a cost of
O(|E|T + NT 2) by the previous arguments.
We should note though that there is potentially one big advantage to using gradients,
which cannot be summarized neatly in asymptotic notation. Since we are performing
all the operations in parallel and on matrices to boot, the task is much easier for most
numerical programming environments such as Matlab and Numpy for Python. While
this advantage becomes less pronounced if we use lower-level languages (although array
operations on adjacent memory locations can still be considerably faster) this also opens
the door for processing everything in parallel on a GPU. This would not be readily
possible for coordinate-ascent since the updates inﬂuence each other.
Working with gradients also gives us the opportunity to reduce the computational
workload by using stochastic approximation.
We note that collapsed gradient updates are no faster, as they have implicitly folded
the model parameter updates into the assignment update, meaning they still have to
do the same counting operations. We will conﬂate collapsed gradient optimization with
standard gradient optimization with regards to the complexity analysis.
Sampled Gradients
Each scalar counting statistic estimator is a simple function of the sample, computable in
O(1), so the complexity is suddenly dominated by the cost of updating the parameters.
We saw that it took only O(T) to update Λ, and O(T 2) to update Ξ.
Again, the
problematic part is the assignment variables Φ. Updating a single set ϕi is only a O(T 2)
operation. But as before we need to do it N times, leaving us at a cost of O(NT 2). On
top of this, this is just for a single gradient sample. If we take S samples we need to
do S times the amount of work, increasing the workload to O(SNT 2). In most cases
though, S will be small and ﬁxed, scaling as O(1).
6.3.5
Total Complexity
If we want to compare coordinate ascent with gradient ascent, we should compare N
low-rank countings with a single full count as per tables 6.1 and 6.2. This leaves the two
algorithms evenly matched with a complexity of O(|E|T + NT 2) in both cases. By using
stochastic updates we can reduce this to O (SNT 2), or simply O (NT 2) if we assume S
to be ﬁxed at O(1). If the graph is dense and |E| scales as O(N 2) the stochastic updates

Scaling in the number of parameters, where every single scalar parameter update is
O(1), is in some sense a lower bound on how well we can do; if we want to do a full pass
over the parameters we need to touch each parameter at least once. Right now we are
held back by each scalar ϕi,k scaling as O(T).
The only way to get below this “bound” is if we can induce more sparsity somehow.
If a parameter update does nothing we can safely skip it. This is in part what Stochastic
Variational Inference as presented in [20] was trying to exploit. Unfortunately, applying
stochastic techniques to the IRM does not result in a similar reduction in computation.
6.4
Validation
Validation has proven troublesome as our algorithms are both modular, stochastic, itera-
tive, somewhat numerically sensitive, and data-dependent. Nevertheless, the algorithms
have undergone thorough validation where possible and some of these eﬀorts are worth
detailing here.
==PAGE:103==
6.4 Validation
95
6.4.1
Validating Natural Gradients
Validating gradients is a common task, and is recommended for e.g. the design of deep
learning systems and other gradient-dependent learning tasks[5]. The most common
tool for this type of validation is the ﬁnite diﬀerence method, which is really just a
computationally intensive numerical approximation of the gradient.
In the limit sense, the gradient is deﬁned as
∂
∂xf(x) = lim
h→0 D(h) = lim
h→0
f(x + h) −f(x)
h
.
(6.18)
Here D(h) is the ﬁnite diﬀerence. For suﬃciently small h, D(h) is going to be indis-
tinguishable from the true gradient
∂
∂xf(x), so we can validate a calculated gradient by
taking small steps in x and checking if the diﬀerence is suﬃciently small.
The ﬁnite diﬀerence formula is rarely used, as there exists a similar formula with
better precision, known as the central diﬀerence.
Dc(h) = f(x + h) −f(x −h)
2h
= 1
2 [D(h) −D(−h)]
(6.19)
So we are taking the average of an estimate of the gradient and the (negative) estimate
of the negative gradient. This is generally the approximation we will use.
Unfortunately, we are not actually working with gradients, but rather the related
natural gradients for which there is no direct ﬁnite diﬀerence scheme. Despite of this,
we can validate the gradients indirectly by using the relationship
ˆ∇f(x) = I−1∇f(x)
where ˆ∇f(x) is the natural gradient and I is the Fisher information matrix, or more
generally the Riemannian metric of the underlying manifold. This connection through
a (locally) linear and non-singular transformation means that we can transform natural
gradients to gradients and vice versa.
This hinges on us knowing the Fisher information matrix, which is the case for all
members of the exponential family in common use. Many of these matrices depend on
special functions such as the polygamma functions though, which potentially introduces
some numerical error into our computations which is only exacerbated by taking the
inverse.
If we write the approximation D(c)(h) =
∂
∂xf(x) + ϵ (the true gradient plus an error
term), then if we apply a linear transformation we will transform both the gradient and
the error term, by way of linearity. This provides us with two ways to compute the
error: either we compute the natural gradient, transform it to a gradient and compare
it to the ﬁnite diﬀerence, or we transform the ﬁnite diﬀerence vector to a “natural ﬁnite
diﬀerence” by multiplying with the inverse Fisher matrix.
Since the matrix scales the error vector, the errors can vary dramatically depending
on what domain we consider them in. If we consider taking the inverse and computing
the ﬁnite diﬀerence to be the most numerically unstable operations, then it follows
==PAGE:104==
96
6 Implementation
that the regular gradient domain is the more stable as we do not need to compute the
diﬀerence.
One ﬁnal consideration is the numerical instability of ﬁnite diﬀerence. When the
steps h get very small the two terms in the numerator might be so close that they
underﬂow to 0. Due to this, it is recommended to use rather large step sizes, all the way
down to 1e −4 [5]. We ﬁnd that the steps around 1e −7 work reasonably well for our
purposes.
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 7
Results
In this chapter we will empirically review and validate some of our propositions from
earlier chapters. All of the algorithms have been personally implemented (and validated)
in Python, and the graphs will be generated from synthetic data or from common real-
world datasets.
We have a few claims we want to verify:
1. Stochastic gradient methods yield a signiﬁcant speedup over coordinate ascent and
standard gradient methods.
2. Choice of learning rate (line search vs. adaptive methods) has an impact.
3. Batch sampling leads to reduced variance.
4. Optimization on the collapsed problem through the KL-corrected is advantageous.
7.1
Performance and Complexity
One of the central goals of this thesis was to speed up inference on the IRM, while ideally
achieving performance of the same order or better. We are particularly interested in
whether our proposed methods scale better to larger graphs. To test this, we generate
a series of diﬀerently sized graphs with between N = 1000 and N = 12000 nodes with
5 clusters each. The structure will be so that each cluster has strong self-connections,
but weak connections to other clusters, yielding an adjacency matrix with a strong block
diagonal. We run each of our algorithms for 30 iterations on each graph and then average
over the timing results (the variance across iterations is usually low).
Figure 7.1 conﬁrms most of our intuitions. Coordinate-ascent scales the worst, as
it has to loop over the latent variables. Gradient methods with the heavier line search
algorithms fare little better as they force the model to do expensive reevaluations for
every trial step size they investigate - also, backtrack is faster than the more meticulous
Wolfe search. Whether the algorithm is working in the collapsed domain or not does not
make a lot of diﬀerence, which makes sense - where the gradient-IRM takes some step
in all directions simultaneously, gradient-CIRM ﬁrst takes a step length of length 1.0 in
all the model parameters (i.e. coordinate-ascent), and then takes a proper gradient step
in the assignment variables. The necessary operations are almost identical.
Stochastic variational inference (SVI), here used in the general sense of stochastic
natural gradient ascent, appears to be the fastest, but the timings are for a single-sample
SVI, so we need to multiply by the number of samples we want. This does not aﬀect

7.2
Line Search and Step Sizes
Picking the right step size is integral for any gradient-based method. We have experi-
mented with a number of strategies, both of the classical kind and some more modern
varieties. We consider how these diﬀerent algorithms match up against each other and
the two diﬀerent cases of deterministic gradients vs. stochastic gradients.

their mutual mean. That is, for each initialization and each time step we compute the
mean between the current objective value of each approach, and then we calculate the
diﬀerence between this mean value and the objective values. One approach can then be

Using this visualization, it appears that Wolfe dominates the backtracking method
consistently in the early steps, but that the backtracking method then overtakes Wolfe
search and converges to a better ﬁnal value. It would appear that it is in some sense
better to take large steps (as backtracking strives to) rather than insisting on getting
nearer to a maximum. As both algorithms appear to converge quickly (which we have
conﬁrmed by running the same simulation for longer), it would appear that Wolfe gets
stuck in less ideal local minima.
While we considered AdaDelta for stochastic inference, which we will also consider
later, it is worth investigating it solely on the virtue of being a low-cost Hessian estimate.
As a Hessian estimate, AdaDelta should be able to take approximate Newton steps
which is the optimal search direction for a locally quadratic function.
Before we get to the comparison, we do a short parameter search for AdaDelta, in
part to verify claims that the method should be insensitive to hyperparameter choice
[58].
As a small reminder, the accumulation factor is the hyperparameter governing the
“width” of the exponentially-weighted running average used to accumulate gradients and
steps. So a high value implies a narrow window and likely a more ﬂuctuating objective
function. Likewise the regularization factor conditions the step size towards length 1
and acts as a surrogate learning rate for the ﬁrst step. If high regularization terms give
good results it is circumstantial evidence that the objective is well-scaled.
So as not to bias the hyperparameter selection too much towards a particular problem
scale we compute two grid searches: one for a small but reasonable graph of 100 nodes
and one for a graph 500 times that. This is of course not enough evidence to make any
==PAGE:109==

global claims about the hyperparameters, but we can at least investigate the sensitivity.
Figures 7.4(a) and 7.4(b) provide some evidence that the problem isn’t hugely sensi-
tive to changes in the hyperparameters, as long as the parameters do not drop too low.
For small problems, it seems to be beneﬁcial to use very large values for the parameters.
That is, a narrow accumulation window and longer steps. As the size of the graph in-
creases, large parameter values become less advantageous. In particular, it appears to
be better to use smaller regularization terms and keep the narrow windows. That we
should use large steps for small problems seems to indicate they are comparatively well-
scaled. A narrow exponential window is then a necessity to keep up with the changes
in curvature. For the larger graph the smaller regularization factor could be indicative
of a less well-scaled problem where longer steps are riskier. That the weighted window
should remain narrow despite taking the algorithm taking smaller steps implies that the
curvature changes more rapidly, which meshes well with our intuition of the problem.
We pick an accumulation factor of 0.1 and a regularization factor of 0.35 for the next
test.
Using this conﬁguration of AdaDelta we can compare runs using backtracking to
runs using AdaDelta. In ﬁgure 7.5, which is on a small graph of 100 nodes, it appears
that they ultimately converge to the same level, but that backtracking converges to
it faster, having a signiﬁcant advantage over AdaDelta for 200 iterations before the
adaptive algorithm closes the gap.

Turning to stochastic gradient methods, we have to discard the otherwise eﬀective line

7.4
Sampling Strategies
Another question we considered was whether we could reduce the variance by construct-
ing alternate sampling schemes. In addition to uniform sampling we considered node-
stratiﬁed sampling [17] and a scheme of our own design dubbed parity-matched sampling.
In our analysis we ended up concluding that neither of the proposed schemes was
likely to improve much on the uniform scheme. Figure 7.10 seems to conﬁrm this. In
fact, despite often taking larger samples stratiﬁed node sampling performs worse than
taking 10 samples uniformly at random. Parity sampling on the other performs on par
with standard uniform sampling, but does not lead to any consistent improvement.

7.6
Clustering
As proof of concept, we want to show that our model both ﬁts the observed graph,
clusters the data in a sensible manner, and is capable of generalizing to unseen data.
First, we need a method of evaluation. While the ELBO is a good measure of ﬁt, it
does not make any claims about unseen data. Instead, we will use two common metrics:
Area under Curve (AUC) and the test log-likelihood.
The AUC is a measure of how accurate our model is as a predictor, but also of how
certain it is in its decisions. It measures the area under the ROC (Receiver Operating
Characteristic) curve, where the ROC curve plots the number of the number of correct
predictions against false positives (wrong guesses).
The curve is a parametric curve
controlled by the threshold at which we classify something as a positive. I.e. if we are
evaluating our ability to predict edges, then if τ is the threshold we would classify eij
as being 1 (positive) when p(eij) > τ. Ideally, the true positive rate outweighs the false
==PAGE:116==
108
7 Results
positive rate meaning that the ROC will lie above the diagonal. This is the motivation
for the AUC score.
To calculate the AUC score, we need to ﬁnd our link predictor p(eij). Conditioning
on the assignment labels, the probability of an edge follows from

7.6.2
Condensed Matter Collaboration Network
As a very brief proof-of-concept, we’ll show that we can also get progress on a large
graph with |V | = 31074 and |E| = 276968 for an approximate edge density of 0.0005.
The network in question is a cleaned and pared down version of the condensed matter
network [37]. We will be using a pared down version of the updated 2005 network, made
to match the vertex set of the 2003 version. We removed all nodes with ambiguous labels
or inconsistent data.
We will use the AUC and log-likelihood score again on a test set of 5000 links and 5000
non-links, all randomly picked from the 2005 graph. Since a proper parameter search is
prohibitively expensive for a graph of this size we settle for a mediocre annealing scheme
with τ = 1000 and κ = 0.75, selected empirically. Performance is likely to better if
the parameters are tuned more rigorously, but it is outside the scope of this thesis. The
algorithm is otherwise identical to the one used for the 10000 node IRM with 10 gradient
samples per iteration and a truncation level of 100.

(b) Log-likelihood of the test set
Figure 7.17: Test statistics for the condensed matter dataset..
Figure 7.17 depicts a very shaky performance sequence. It appears that the gradient
directions followed for the ﬁrst 1000 iterations are in line with the test set, but then
performance starts taking a dive in both statistics. Assuming the ﬁrst 1000 steps were
spent reaching a region of good solutions respective to the entire dataset, it makes sense
that the uncertainty and noise of the gradient estimate increases as diﬀerent samples
start pulling in diﬀerent directions, making it less likely that they are increasing predic-
tion performance for the test set. We note that the AUC in particular exhibits strong
noise in this particular interval.
The dive in AUC is likely due to sample noise or overshooting a stationary point and
is followed by rather consistent increase in performance.
It should be noted that with the relatively small truncation level we are operating at,
huge clusters are fairly likely. If our test points rest in a big cluster with a high probabil-
==PAGE:120==
112
7 Results
ity, then they are modelled little better than by an Erdos-Renyi graph model, and one
dominated by thousands of other points. So improvements in the global performance
can easily be misrepresented in the test statistics.
==PAGE:121==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
This thesis started with the goal of applying stochastic variational inference, as presented
in [20], to the inﬁnite relational model.
We broke SVI down into its basic components, namely variational inference, expo-
nential family distributions, natural gradients and stochastic approximation methods,
and exploitation of conditional independence structure. We described in some detail
how all of these components work and how they can be combined to perform stochastic
variational inference in the sense employed in [20].
While most of the components were easily transferable to the IRM, we showed that
while the IRM is superﬁcially simpler than the assortative mixed-membership model of
Gopalan et al. [17], it does not have the necessary global-local structure to bring the full
power of the SVI approach to bear.
With classic SVI inapplicable, we considered alternate approaches involving stochas-
tic approximation. Building on the same natural gradient optimization method applied
in SVI, we developed a more conventional stochastic inference procedure that neverthe-
less brought the computational time down from O(|E|T + NT 2) to O(NT 2).
As a complete alternative to stochastic approximation, we considered exploiting the
global-local independence structure in another way by doing away with the model pa-
rameters entirely. This led us to collapsed Bayesian models where we explored a recent
innovation called the KL-corrected variational bound. We applied this to the IRM and
observed marginal gains by allowing gradient optimization algorithms to take larger steps
and exploit an increased curvature. Unfortunately, the KL bound was less amenable to
a stochastic treatment, so there was computationally little advantage to be gained.
Taking inspiration from [17], we also explored the idea of alternative sampling schemes
to reduce the variance of the gradient estimates. We developed a new framework for
analyzing the variance of these sampling schemes, which unfortunately led us to the con-
clusion that while variance-reducing sampling schemes are theoretically possible, none
of the strategies we tried to pursue could boast a variance reduction better than simple
uniform sampling. This could be a possible direction for future research.
So, in short
• We showed that SVI cannot be applied readily to the inﬁnite relational model
• We developed a stochastic approximation scheme for the IRM and demonstrated
both empirically and theoretically how it has a lower computational complexity
than traditional methods of inference.
• We gave a thorough analysis of the KL-corrected bound and provided empirical
evidence that the collapsed IRM has a performance advantage.
==PAGE:122==
114
8 Conclusion
• We developed design guidelines for variance-reducing sampling schemes.
Future Work
We consider some possible future directions for this work.
Optimization
While the improvement in computational complexity is marginal unless the graph is
dense, the natural gradient framework allows us to escape the rather constrictive vari-
ational Bayes/coordinate ascent setup. For increased speed, future work could explore
parallelized implementations or more advanced ﬁrst or second-order methods of opti-
mization such as conjugate gradient descent or BFGS [42]. In particular, Honkela et al.
explored using natural gradients in a conjugate gradient scheme [21]. We brieﬂy inves-
tigated this, but did not have time to properly explore the topic. Another thing that
could be done to ensure better performance is to work on initializing the model properly,
to guard against poor local optima and poor convergence behavior. One eﬀort towards
this is an annealing scheme for variational inference, pioneered in [29] and later extended
to SVI [1].
Variance Reduction
The next big problem is the high variance of the gradients which is also the primary
problem faced by Black-box gradients [45]. A next step might be to develop a control-
variate for the IRM gradient or simply use the one proposed by [45]. We also feel that
the sampling strategies have some potential as a future ﬁeld of study. While we ended
up deciding it was a bit too tangential to include in the thesis, we also explored a recent
variational procedure developed by Salimans and Knowles called stochastic regression
which boasts low noise and high performance [47, 48].
Independence Structure
While the IRM turned out to be a poor ﬁt for SVI, it is possible that it could be modiﬁed
to have a compliant global-local structure. One could imagine a “noisy” IRM where each
edge only receives the right label some percentage of the time, which would create a local
edge context.
Exchangeability
The idea of exchangeability could perhaps also be pursued further. The counting statis-
tics are suﬃcient statistics for the IRM, so we dabbled in building them into the model
in a probabilistic sense through collective graphical models [53], but our eﬀorts were cut
short again by the dependence structure of the IRM. Recent research has also shown
how exchangeability can be directly applied in probabilistic inference [40, 41] and we
have even seen examples of these ideas applied to simplify variational inference [10].

Figure A.1: A grid search run on 50 independent instances of 500 node graphs with
100 iterations. Darker values are better..
When matching the annealing scheme against AdaDelta we need to ﬁnd a good setting
for the hyperparameters of the annealing scheme to make the comparison fair. Since the
annealing scheme is of secondary importance to this thesis, we have relegated the grid
search to the appendix.
From ﬁgure A.1 we note a dark band of good values across the grid, ruling out only
high forgetting rate coupled with low delay (leading to a very rapid decrease in learning
rate and slow progress) and a low forgetting rate coupled with high delay (leading to
slow decrease and a long period with near constant rate). Based on these results we
settle for a safe compromise of τ = 5500 and τ = 0.7.

269
'''Initialize assignment variables so they put 'dominance'

270

405 def clusterplot(axis, graph, clustering , color='gist_rainbow', backfill =
False):
406
'''Sort graph according to 'clustering' and color
each clustered
block.'''
407
colorful = plt.get_cmap(color)
408

black
409

#current best clustering
411

412

#Produce coloring
414

415

416

417

418

419

interpolation=None)
425
else:
426
modest_image.imshow(axis,reordered.multiply(coloring),vmin=1e-3,
vmax=1,cmap=colorful ,interpolation=None)

def count(self):

'''Compute counting statistics.

def recount(self,index,change):

def meanfield_assignment(self, index = None, sample = None):
304
'''Compute coordinate ascent update for assignment variables using
CVB0 assumption'''
305
assert self.model.do_count
306
membership = self.model.get_membership(sample) - self.model.
expected_assignments[index]
307
membership_above = membership.sum() - membership.cumsum(sample)
308
309
310
normalizer = np.log(1.0 + membership + self.model.alpha +
membership_above)
311
member_factor = np.log(1.0 + membership) - normalizer
312
nomember_factor = np.log(self.model.alpha + membership_above) -
normalizer
313
member_contribution = member_factor + nomember_factor.cumsum() -
nomember_factor
314
315
connectivity_count , noconnectivity_count = self.model.
get_both_connectivity_counts(sample)
316
317

connectivity_count[index])

interaction_i += interaction_i.T
320
scalediagonal(interaction_i , 0.5)
321
322
nointeraction_full = self.model.get_nointeraction(sample)
323
nointeraction_i = np.outer(self.model.expected_assignments[index],
noconnectivity_count[index])

nointeraction_i += nointeraction_i.T

scalediagonal(nointeraction_i , 0.5)
326

interaction_i ,
328
self.model.beta + nointeraction_full - nointeraction_i)

330
self.model.beta + nointeraction_full) - cluster_normalizer

cluster_contribution.sum(0))

logproposal[...,-1]
